<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">International System of Units</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Modern form of the metric system</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"SI" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Si_(disambiguation)" title="Si (disambiguation)">Si (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p>The  <b>International System of Units,</b> known by the international abbreviation <b>SI</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[a]</a></sup> in all languages<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 125">: 125 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST330_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIST330-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: iii">: iii </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_SI_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_SI-4">[3]</a></sup> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Pleonasm#Acronyms_and_initialisms" title="Pleonasm">pleonastically</a> as the <b>SI system</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[b]</a></sup> is the modern form<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 117">: 117 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Introduction_to_Statistics_in_Metrology_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Introduction_to_Statistics_in_Metrology-8">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_Metric_system_America_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian_Metric_system_America-9">[7]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric system</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[g]</a></sup> and the world's most widely used <a href="/wiki/System_of_measurement" title="System of measurement">system of measurement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 123">: 123 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curtin_Distance_in_McColl_Encyclopedia_of_World_Geography_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curtin_Distance_in_McColl_Encyclopedia_of_World_Geography-16">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPL_Measure_of_all_things_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPL_Measure_of_all_things-17">[10]</a></sup>  Established and maintained<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FR_Interpretation_of_the_SI_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FR_Interpretation_of_the_SI-18">[11]</a></sup> by the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGPM_briefly_explained_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-CGPM_briefly_explained-23">[j]</a></sup> (CGPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_CGPM_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-French_CGPM-24">[k]</a></sup>), it is the only system of measurement with an official status<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[m]</a></sup> in nearly every country in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[n]</a></sup> employed in science, technology, industry, and everyday commerce.
</p><p>The SI comprises a <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(units_of_measurement)" title="Coherence (units of measurement)">coherent</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[o]</a></sup> system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" title="Units of measurement">units of measurement</a> starting with seven <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">base units</a>, which are the <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a> (symbol s, the unit of <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>), <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a> (m, <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a>), <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> (kg, <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> (A, <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>), <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> (K, <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> (mol, <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">candela</a> (cd, <a href="/wiki/Luminous_intensity" title="Luminous intensity">luminous intensity</a>). The system can accommodate coherent units for an unlimited number of additional quantities. These are called coherent <a href="/wiki/SI_derived_unit" title="SI derived unit">derived units</a>, which can always be represented as products of powers of the base units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[p]</a></sup> Twenty-two coherent derived units have been provided with special names and symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22derived_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-22derived-30">[q]</a></sup>
</p><p>The seven base units and the 22 coherent derived units with special names and symbols may be used in combination to express other coherent derived units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29combin_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-29combin-31">[r]</a></sup> Since the sizes of coherent units will be convenient for only some applications and not for others, the SI provides twenty <a href="/wiki/Metric_prefix" title="Metric prefix">prefixes</a> which, when added to the name and symbol of a coherent unit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[s]</a></sup> produce twenty additional (non-coherent) SI units for the same quantity; these non-coherent units are always decimal (i.e. power-of-ten) multiples and sub-multiples of the coherent unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[t]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[u]</a></sup> The SI is intended to be an evolving system; units and prefixes are created and unit definitions are modified through international agreement as the technology of <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurement</a> progresses and the precision of measurements improves.
</p><p>Since 2019, the magnitudes of all SI units have been defined by declaring that seven <i>defining constants</i> have certain exact numerical values when expressed in terms of their SI units. These defining constants are the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Caesium_standard" title="Caesium standard">hyperfine transition frequency of caesium</a> <span class="texhtml">Δ<i>ν</i><sub>Cs</sub></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i><sub>A</sub></span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">luminous efficacy</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i><sub>cd</sub></span>. The nature of the defining constants ranges from fundamental constants of nature such as <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> to the purely technical constant <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i><sub>cd</sub></span>. Prior to 2019, <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i><sub>A</sub></span> were not defined a priori but were rather very precisely measured quantities. In 2019, their values were fixed by definition to their best estimates at the time, ensuring continuity with previous definitions of the base units.
</p><p>The current way of defining the SI is a result of a decades-long move towards increasingly abstract and idealised formulation in which the <a href="/wiki/Realisation_(metrology)" title="Realisation (metrology)">realisations</a> of the units are separated conceptually from the definitions. A consequence is that as science and technologies develop, new and superior realisations may be introduced without the need to redefine the unit. One problem with artefacts is that they can be lost, damaged, or changed; another is that they introduce uncertainties that cannot be reduced by advancements in science and technology. The last artefact used by the SI was the <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_of_the_Kilogram" title="International Prototype of the Kilogram">International Prototype of the Kilogram</a>, a cylinder of <a href="/wiki/Platinum%E2%80%93iridium_alloy" title="Platinum–iridium alloy">platinum–iridium</a>.
</p><p>The original motivation for the development of the SI was the diversity of units that had sprung up within the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">centimetre–gram–second</a> (CGS) systems (specifically the inconsistency between the systems of <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_units" title="Electrostatic units">electrostatic units</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_units" title="Electromagnetic units">electromagnetic units</a>) and the lack of coordination between the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" title="Discipline (academia)">disciplines</a> that used them. The <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (French: <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Conférence générale des poids et mesures</i></span></i> – CGPM), which was established by the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a> of 1875, brought together many international organisations to establish the definitions and standards of a new system and to standardise the rules for writing and presenting measurements. The system was published in 1960 as a result of an initiative that began in 1948, so it is based on the <a href="/wiki/MKS_system_of_units" title="MKS system of units">metre–kilogram–second system of units</a> (MKS) rather than any variant of the CGS.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:281px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metric_and_imperial_systems_(2019).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Metric_and_imperial_systems_%282019%29.svg/279px-Metric_and_imperial_systems_%282019%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Metric_and_imperial_systems_%282019%29.svg/419px-Metric_and_imperial_systems_%282019%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Metric_and_imperial_systems_%282019%29.svg/558px-Metric_and_imperial_systems_%282019%29.svg.png 2x" width="279"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metric_and_imperial_systems_(2019).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries using the <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric</a> (SI), <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_customary" title="US customary">US customary</a> systems as of 2019.</div></div></div>
<p>The International System of Units, or SI,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title='Page / location: 123
Quotation : "... since its establishment in 1960, the International System of Units has always been referred to as "the SI" in its shortened form."'>123</span> </sup> is a decimal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[v]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[w]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/System_of_units" title="System of units">system of units</a> established in 1960 and periodically updated since then. The SI has an <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">official status</a> in most countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[x]</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_the_United_States" title="Metrication in the United States">the United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[y]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_Canada" title="Metrication in Canada">Canada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Metrication in the United Kingdom">the United Kingdom</a>, although these three countries are amongst a handful of nations that, to various degrees, also continue to use their customary systems. Nevertheless, with this nearly universal level of acceptance, the SI "has been used around the world as the preferred system of units, the basic language for science, technology, industry and trade."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 123">: 123 </span></sup>
</p><p>The only other types of measurement system that still have widespread use across the world are the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_and_US_customary_measurement_systems" title="Imperial and US customary measurement systems">Imperial and US customary measurement systems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[z]</a></sup> and they are legally <a href="/wiki/International_yard_and_pound" title="International yard and pound">defined in terms of the SI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[aa]</a></sup> There are other, less widespread systems of measurement that are occasionally used in particular regions of the world. In addition, there are many individual non-SI units that don't belong to any comprehensive system of units, but that are nevertheless still regularly used in particular fields and regions. Both of these categories of unit are also typically defined legally in terms of SI units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[ab]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controlling_body">Controlling body</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Controlling body">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The SI was established and is maintained by the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (CGPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_CGPM_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-French_CGPM-24">[k]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FR_Interpretation_of_the_SI_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-FR_Interpretation_of_the_SI-18">[11]</a></sup> In practice, the CGPM follows the recommendations of the Consultative Committee for Units (CCU), which is the actual body conducting technical deliberations concerning new scientific and technological developments related to the definition of units and the SI. The CCU reports to  the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Committee_for_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Committee for Weights and Measures">International Committee for Weights and Measures</a> (CIPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_CIPM_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-French_CIPM-44">[ac]</a></sup>), which, in turn, reports to the CGPM. See <a href="#Controlling_authority">below</a> for more details.
</p><p>All the decisions and recommendations concerning units are collected in a brochure called <a href="#SI_Brochure"><i>The International System of Units (SI)</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_Brochure_short_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-SI_Brochure_short-45">[ad]</a></sup> which is published by the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_from_French_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_from_French-46">[ae]</a></sup>) and periodically updated.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview_of_the_units">Overview of the units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Overview of the units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SI_base_units">SI base units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: SI base units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The SI selects seven units to serve as <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">base units</a>, corresponding to seven base physical quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[af]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[ag]</a></sup> They are the <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a>, with the symbol <span class="nowrap">s</span>, which is the SI unit of the physical quantity of <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a>, symbol <span class="nowrap">m</span>, the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Length" title="Length">length</a>; <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> (<span class="nowrap">kg</span>, the unit of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>); <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a> (<span class="nowrap">A</span>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>); <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> (<span class="nowrap">K</span>, <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a>); <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> (<span class="nowrap">mol</span>, <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">candela</a> (<span class="nowrap">cd</span>, <a href="/wiki/Luminous_intensity" title="Luminous intensity">luminous intensity</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup> All units in the SI can be expressed in terms of the base units, and the base units serve as a preferred set for expressing or analysing the relationships between units.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SI_derived_units">SI derived units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: SI derived units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The system allows for an unlimited number of additional units, called <a href="/wiki/SI_derived_unit" title="SI derived unit"><i>derived units</i></a>, which can always be represented as products of powers of the base units, possibly with a nontrivial numeric multiplier. When that multiplier is one, the unit is called a <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(units_of_measurement)" title="Coherence (units of measurement)"><i>coherent</i></a> derived unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[ah]</a></sup> The base and coherent derived units of the SI together form a coherent system of units (<i>the set of coherent SI units</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[ai]</a></sup> Twenty-two coherent derived units have been provided with special names and symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22derived_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-22derived-30">[q]</a></sup> The seven base units and the 22 derived units with special names and symbols may be used in combination to express other derived units,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29combin_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-29combin-31">[r]</a></sup> which are adopted to facilitate measurement of diverse quantities.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Why_SI_kept_the_distinction_between_base_and_derived_units">Why SI kept the distinction between base and derived units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Why SI kept the distinction between base and derived units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Prior to its redefinition in 2019, the SI was defined through the seven base units from which the derived units were constructed as products of powers of the base units. After the redefinition, the SI is defined by fixing the numerical values of seven defining constants. This has the effect that the distinction between the base units and derived units is, in principle, not needed, since all units, base as well as derived, may be constructed directly from the defining constants. Nevertheless, the distinction is retained because 'it is useful and historically well established', and also because the <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_80000" title="ISO/IEC 80000">ISO/IEC 80000</a> series of standards<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[aj]</a></sup> specifies base and derived quantities that necessarily have the corresponding SI units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 129">: 129 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SI_metric_prefixes_and_the_decimal_nature_of_the_SI">SI metric prefixes and the decimal nature of the SI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: SI metric prefixes and the decimal nature of the SI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Like all metric systems, the SI uses <a href="/wiki/Metric_prefix" title="Metric prefix">metric prefixes</a> to systematically construct, for the same physical quantity, a set of units that are decimal multiples of each other over a wide range.
</p><p>For example, while the coherent unit of length is the metre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[ak]</a></sup> the SI provides a full range of smaller and larger units of length, any of which may be more convenient for any given application – for example, driving distances are normally given in <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">kilometres</a> (symbol <span class="nowrap">km</span>) rather than in metres. Here the metric prefix '<a href="/wiki/Kilo-" title="Kilo-">kilo-</a>' (symbol 'k') stands for a factor of 1000; thus, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003100000000000000♠"></span>1 km</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003100000000000000♠"></span>1000 m</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[al]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current version of the SI provides twenty metric prefixes that signify decimal powers ranging from  10<sup>−24</sup> to 10<sup>24</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 143–4">: 143–4 </span></sup> Most prefixes correspond to integer powers of 1000; the only ones that do not are those for 10, 1/10, 100, and 1/100.
</p><p>In general, given any coherent unit with a separate name and symbol,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[am]</a></sup> one forms a new unit by simply adding an appropriate metric prefix to the name of the coherent unit (and a corresponding prefix symbol to the coherent unit's symbol).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prefixkg_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-prefixkg-56">[an]</a></sup> Since the metric prefix signifies a particular power of ten, the new unit is always a power-of-ten multiple or sub-multiple of the coherent unit. Thus, the conversion between different SI units for one and the same physical quantity is always through a power of ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[ao]</a></sup> This is why the SI (and metric systems more generally) are called <i>decimal systems of measurement units</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DecimalSystem_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-DecimalSystem-58">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[ap]</a></sup>
</p><p>The grouping formed by a prefix symbol attached to a unit symbol (e.g. '<span class="nowrap">km</span>', '<span class="nowrap">cm</span>') constitutes a new inseparable unit symbol. This new symbol can be raised to a positive or negative power and can be combined with other unit symbols to form compound unit symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 143">: 143 </span></sup> For example, <span class="nowrap">g/cm<sup>3</sup></span> is an SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, where <span class="nowrap">cm<sup>3</sup></span> is to be interpreted as (<span class="nowrap">cm</span>)<sup>3</sup>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coherent_and_non-coherent_SI_units">Coherent and non-coherent SI units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Coherent and non-coherent SI units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When prefixes are used with the coherent SI units, the resulting units are no longer coherent, because the prefix introduces a numerical factor other than one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 137">: 137 </span></sup> The one exception is the kilogram, the only coherent SI unit whose name and symbol, for historical reasons, include a prefix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prefixkg_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-prefixkg-56">[an]</a></sup>
</p><p>The complete set of SI units consists of both the coherent set and the multiples and sub-multiples of coherent units formed by using the SI prefixes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 138">: 138 </span></sup> For example, the metre, kilometre, centimetre, nanometre, etc. are all SI units of length, though only the metre is a <i>coherent</i> SI unit. A similar statement holds for derived units: for example, <span class="nowrap">kg/m<sup>3</sup></span>, <span class="nowrap">g/dm<sup>3</sup></span>, <span class="nowrap">g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petagram" title="Petagram">Pg</a></span>/<span class="nowrap">km<sup>3</sup></span>, etc. are all SI units of density, but of these, only <span class="nowrap">kg/m<sup>3</sup></span> is a <i>coherent</i> SI unit.
</p><p>Moreover, the metre is the <i>only</i> coherent SI unit of length. Every physical quantity has exactly one coherent SI unit, although this unit may be expressible in different forms by using some of the special names and symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 140">: 140 </span></sup> For example, the coherent SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">linear momentum</a> may be written as either <span class="nowrap">kg⋅m/s</span> or as <span class="nowrap">N⋅s</span>, and both forms are in use (e.g. compare respectively here<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atkins_Dictionary_of_Mechanical_Engineering_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins_Dictionary_of_Mechanical_Engineering-60">[18]</a></sup><sup>:<a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/books/edition/A_Dictionary_of_Mechanical_Engineering/cutcBgAAQBAJ?&amp;gbpv=1&amp;pg=PA205&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;bsq=%22linear%20momentum%20(unit%20kg.m%2Fs)%22" rel="nofollow">205</a></sup> and here<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapple_Dictionary_of_Physics_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapple_Dictionary_of_Physics-61">[19]</a></sup><sup>:<a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/books/edition/Dictionary_of_Physics/qfdRAwAAQBAJ?&amp;gbpv=1&amp;pg=PT141&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;bsq=%22the%20SI%20unit%20for%20linear%20momentum%2C%20the%20newton%20second%22" rel="nofollow">135</a></sup>).
</p><p>On the other hand, several different quantities may share same coherent SI unit.  For example, the joule per kelvin (symbol <span class="nowrap">J/K</span>) is the coherent SI unit for two distinct quantities: <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a>; another example is the ampere, which is the coherent SI unit for both <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetomotive_force" title="Magnetomotive force">magnetomotive force</a>. This is why it is important not to use the unit alone to specify the quantity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[aq]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, the same coherent SI unit may be a base unit in one context, but a coherent derived unit in another. For example, the ampere is a base unit when it is a unit of electric current, but a coherent derived unit when it is a unit of magnetomotive force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-19"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 140">: 140 </span></sup> As perhaps a more familiar example, consider <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">rainfall</a>, defined as volume of rain (measured in <span class="nowrap">m<sup>3</sup></span>) that fell per unit area (measured in <span class="nowrap">m<sup>2</sup></span>). Since <span class="nowrap">m<sup>3</sup></span>/<span class="nowrap">m<sup>2</sup></span> = <span class="nowrap">m</span>, it follows that the coherent <i>derived</i> SI unit of rainfall is the metre, even though the metre is also the <i>base</i> SI unit of length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[ar]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Permitted_non-SI_units">Permitted non-SI units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Permitted non-SI units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There is a special group of units that are called "non-SI units that are accepted for use with the SI".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-20"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 145">: 145 </span></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Non-SI_units_mentioned_in_the_SI" title="Non-SI units mentioned in the SI">Non-SI units mentioned in the SI</a> for a full list. Most of these, in order to be converted to the corresponding SI unit, require conversion factors that are not powers of ten. Some common examples of such units are the customary units of time, namely the minute (conversion factor of 60 s/min, since 1 min = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001600000000000000♠"></span>60 s</span>), the hour (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003360000000000000♠"></span>3600 s</span>), and the day (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004864000000000000♠"></span>86<span style="margin-left:.25em;">400</span> s</span>); the degree (for measuring plane angles, <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6998174532925199433♠"></span>1°</span> = <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml"><i>π</i></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">180</span></span> <span class="nowrap">rad</span>);</span> and the <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">electronvolt</a> (a unit of energy, <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6981160217648700000♠"></span>1 eV</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6981160217663400000♠"></span>1.602<span style="margin-left:.25em;">176</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">634</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−19</sup> J</span>).</span>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_units">New units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: New units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The SI is intended to be an evolving system; units<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[as]</a></sup> and prefixes are created and unit definitions are modified through international agreement as the technology of <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measurement</a> progresses and the precision of measurements improves.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_magnitudes_of_units">Defining magnitudes of units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Defining magnitudes of units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since 2019, the magnitudes of all SI units have been defined in an abstract way, which is conceptually separated from any practical realisation of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-22"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 126">: 126 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[at]</a></sup> Namely, the SI units are defined by declaring that seven <i>defining constants</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-23"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 125–9">: 125–9 </span></sup> have certain exact numerical values when expressed in terms of their SI units. Probably the most widely known of these constants is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum, <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span>, which in the SI by definition has the exact value of <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span> m/s</span>. The other six constants are <span class="texhtml">Δ<i>ν</i><sub>Cs</sub></span>,  the <a href="/wiki/Caesium_standard" title="Caesium standard">hyperfine transition frequency of caesium</a>; <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>; <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a>; <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>; <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i></span><sub>A</sub>, the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a>; and <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i></span><sub>cd</sub>, the <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">luminous efficacy</a> of monochromatic radiation of frequency <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7014540000000000000♠"></span>540<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>12</sup> Hz</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[au]</a></sup> The nature of the defining constants ranges from fundamental constants of nature such as <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> to the purely technical constant <span class="texhtml"><i>K</i></span><sub>cd</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-25"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 128–9">: 128–9 </span></sup> Prior to 2019, <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i></span><sub>A</sub> were not defined a priori but were rather very precisely measured quantities. In 2019, their values were fixed by definition to their best estimates at the time, ensuring continuity with previous definitions of the base units.
</p><p>As far as realisations, what are believed to be the current best practical realisations of units are described in the <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">mises en pratique</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[av]</a></sup> which are also published by the BIPM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MisesEnPratique_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-MisesEnPratique-70">[22]</a></sup>  The abstract nature of the definitions of units is what makes it possible to improve and change the <i>mises en pratique</i> as science and technology develop without having to change the actual definitions themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[ay]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a sense, this way of defining the SI units is no more abstract than the way derived units are traditionally defined in terms of the base units. Consider a particular derived unit, for example, the joule, the unit of energy. Its definition in terms of the base units is <span class="nowrap">kg</span>⋅<span class="nowrap">m<sup>2</sup></span>/<span class="nowrap">s<sup>2</sup></span>. Even if the practical realisations of the metre, kilogram, and second are available, a practical realisation of the joule would require some sort of reference to the underlying physical definition of work or energy—some actual physical procedure for realising the energy in the amount of one joule such that it can be compared to other instances of energy (such as the energy content of gasoline put into a car or of electricity delivered to a household).
</p><p>The situation with the defining constants and all of the SI units is analogous. In fact, purely <i>mathematically</i> speaking, the SI units are defined <i>as if</i> we declared that it is the defining constant's units that are now the base units, with all other SI units being derived units. To make this clearer, first note that each defining constant can be taken as determining the magnitude of that defining constant's unit of measurement;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-26"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 128">: 128 </span></sup> for example, the definition of <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span> defines the unit <span class="nowrap">m/s</span> as <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 m/s</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span></span></span></span> ('the speed of one metre per second is equal to one <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span></span>th of the speed of light'). In this way, the defining constants directly define the following seven units: 
</p>
<div><ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a> (<span class="nowrap">Hz</span>), a unit of the physical quantity of frequency;</li><li>the <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">metre per second</a> (<span class="nowrap">m/s</span>), a unit of speed;</li><li>the <a href="/wiki/Joule-second" title="Joule-second">joule-second</a> (<span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>), a unit of <a href="/wiki/Action_(physics)" title="Action (physics)">action</a>;</li><li>the <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">coulomb</a> (<span class="nowrap">C</span>), a unit of <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a>;</li><li>the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joule_per_kelvin" title="Joule per kelvin">joule per kelvin</a> (<span class="nowrap">J/K</span>), a unit of both <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a>;</li><li>the inverse <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> (<span class="nowrap">mol<sup>−1</sup></span>), a unit of a conversion constant between the <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a> and the number of elementary entities (atoms, molecules, etc.);</li><li>and the <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">lumen</a> per <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watt</a> (<span class="nowrap">lm/W</span>), a unit of <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">luminous efficacy</a> (conversion constant between the physical power carried by electromagnetic radiation and the intrinsic ability of that same radiation to produce visual perception of brightness in humans).</li></ul></div>
<p>Further, one can show, using <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimensional analysis</a>, that every coherent SI unit (whether base or derived) can be written as a unique product of powers of the units of the SI defining constants (in complete analogy to the fact that every coherent derived SI unit can be written as a unique product of powers of the base SI units). For example, the kilogram can be written as <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap">kg</span> = (<span class="nowrap">Hz</span>)(<span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>)/(<span class="nowrap">m/s</span>)<sup>2</sup>.</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[az]</a></sup> Thus, the kilogram is defined in terms of the three defining constants <span class="texhtml">Δ<i>ν</i><sub>Cs</sub></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span> because, on the one hand, these three defining constants respectively define the units <span class="nowrap">Hz</span>, <span class="nowrap">m/s</span>, and <span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[ba]</a></sup> while, on the other hand, the kilogram can be written in terms of these three units, namely, <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap">kg</span> = (<span class="nowrap">Hz</span>)(<span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>)/(<span class="nowrap">m/s</span>)<sup>2</sup>.</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[bb]</a></sup> While the question of how to actually realise the kilogram in practice would, at this point, still be open, that is not really different from the fact that the question of how to actually realise the joule in practice is still in principle open even once one has achieved the practical realisations of the metre, kilogram, and second.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifying_fundamental_constants_vs._other_methods_of_definition">Specifying fundamental constants vs. other methods of definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Specifying fundamental constants vs. other methods of definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The current way of defining the SI is the result of a decades-long move towards increasingly abstract and idealised formulation in which the
<a href="/wiki/Realisation_(metrology)" title="Realisation (metrology)">realisations</a> of the units are separated conceptually from the definitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-28"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 126">: 126 </span></sup>
</p><p>The great advantage of doing it this way is that as science and technologies develop, new and superior realisations may be introduced without the need to redefine the units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Second_as_exception_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Second_as_exception-71">[aw]</a></sup>  Units can now be realised with 'an accuracy that is ultimately limited only by the quantum structure of nature and our technical abilities but not by the definitions themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant-72">[ax]</a></sup> Any valid equation of physics relating the defining constants to a unit can be used to realise the unit, thus creating opportunities for innovation... with increasing accuracy as technology proceeds.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-29"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 122">: 122 </span></sup> In practice, the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures#Consultative_committees" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">CIPM Consultative Committees</a> provide so-called "<i>mises en pratique</i><span style="padding-left:.15em;">"</span> (practical techniques),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MisesEnPratique_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-MisesEnPratique-70">[22]</a></sup> which are the descriptions of what are currently believed to be best experimental realisations of the units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WhatIsMiseEnPratique_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-WhatIsMiseEnPratique-80">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>This system lacks the conceptual simplicity of using artefacts (referred to as <i>prototypes</i>) as realisations of units to define those units: with prototypes, the definition and the realisation are one and the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[bc]</a></sup> However, using artefacts has two major disadvantages that, as soon as it is technologically and scientifically feasible, result in abandoning them as means for defining units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[bg]</a></sup> One major disadvantage is that artefacts can be lost, damaged,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834-92">[bi]</a></sup> or changed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[bj]</a></sup> The other is that they largely cannot benefit from advancements in science and technology. The last artefact used by the SI was the <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_of_the_Kilogram" title="International Prototype of the Kilogram">International Prototype Kilogram</a> (IPK), a particular cylinder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platinum-iridium_alloy" title="Platinum-iridium alloy">platinum-iridium</a>; from 1889 to 2019, the kilogram was by definition equal to the mass of the IPK. Concerns <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_of_the_Kilogram#Stability_of_the_IPK" title="International Prototype of the Kilogram">regarding its stability</a> on the one hand, and  <a href="/wiki/Alternative_approaches_to_redefining_the_kilogram" title="Alternative approaches to redefining the kilogram">progress in precise measurements of the Planck constant and the Avogadro constant</a> on the other, led to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2019_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="2019 redefinition of SI base units">revision of the definition of the base units</a>, put into effect on 20 May 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_2018-11_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIST_2018-11-95">[33]</a></sup> This was the biggest change in the SI since it was first formally defined and established in 1960, and it resulted in the definitions described above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the past, there were also various other approaches to the definitions of some of the SI units. One made use of a specific physical state of a specific substance (the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin#History" title="Kelvin">triple point of water</a>, which was used in the definition of the kelvin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 113–4">: 113–4 </span></sup>); others referred to idealised experimental prescriptions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-31"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 125">: 125 </span></sup> (as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Ampere#Former_definition_in_the_SI" title="Ampere">former SI definition of the ampere</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 113">: 113 </span></sup> and the former SI definition (originally enacted in 1979) <a href="/wiki/Candela#History" title="Candela">of the candela</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 115">: 115 </span></sup>).
</p><p>In the future, the set of defining constants used by the SI may be modified as more stable constants are found, or if it turns out that other constants can be more precisely measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[bk]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The original motivation for the development of the SI was the diversity of units that had sprung up within the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">centimetre–gram–second</a> (CGS) systems (specifically the inconsistency between the systems of <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_units" title="Electrostatic units">electrostatic units</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_units" title="Electromagnetic units">electromagnetic units</a>) and the lack of coordination between the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discipline_(academia)" title="Discipline (academia)">disciplines</a> that used them. The <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (French: <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Conférence générale des poids et mesures</i></span></i> – CGPM), which was established by the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a> of 1875, brought together many international organisations to establish the definitions and standards of a new system and to standardise the rules for writing and presenting measurements.
</p><p>Adopted in 1889, use of the MKS system of units succeeded the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">centimetre–gram–second system of units</a> (CGS) in <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>. The metre and kilogram system served as the basis for the development of the International System of Units (abbreviated SI), which now serves as the international standard. Because of this, the standards of the CGS system were gradually replaced with metric standards incorporated from the MKS system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1901, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Giorgi" title="Giovanni Giorgi">Giovanni Giorgi</a> proposed to the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Associazione_elettrotecnica_italiana&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Associazione elettrotecnica italiana (page does not exist)">Associazione elettrotecnica italiana</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Associazione_elettrotecnica_italiana" title="it:Associazione elettrotecnica italiana">it</a>]</span> (AEI) that this system, extended with a fourth unit to be taken from the units of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, be used as an international system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[37]</a></sup>    
This system was strongly promoted by electrical engineer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_A._Campbell" title="George A. Campbell">George A. Campbell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brainerd_1970_p=601_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brainerd_1970_p=601-101">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The International System was published in 1960, based on the MKS units, as a result of an initiative that began in 1948.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controlling_authority">Controlling authority</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Controlling authority">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The SI is regulated and continually developed by three international organisations that were established in 1875 under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>. They are the <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (CGPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_CGPM_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-French_CGPM-24">[k]</a></sup>), the International Committee for Weights and Measures (CIPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_CIPM_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-French_CIPM-44">[ac]</a></sup>), and the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_from_French_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_from_French-46">[ae]</a></sup>). The ultimate authority rests with the CGPM, which is a plenary body through which its Member States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Member_States_of_CGPM_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Member_States_of_CGPM-102">[bl]</a></sup> act together on matters related to measurement science and measurement standards; it usually convenes every four years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_CGPM_Members_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_CGPM_Members-21">[12]</a></sup> The CGPM elects the CIPM, which is an 18-person committee of eminent scientists. The CIPM operates based on the advice of a number of its Consultative Committees, which bring together the world's experts in their specified fields as advisers on scientific and technical matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_Consultative_Committees_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_Consultative_Committees-103">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[bm]</a></sup> One of these committees is the Consultative Committee for Units (CCU), which is responsible for <i>matters related to the development of the International System of Units (SI), preparation of successive editions of the SI brochure, and advice to the CIPM on matters concerning units of measurement.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_CCU_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_CCU-105">[40]</a></sup> It is the CCU which considers in detail all new scientific and technological developments related to the definition of units and the SI. In practice, when it comes to the definition of the SI, the CGPM simply formally approves the recommendations of the CIPM, which, in turn, follows the advice of the CCU.
</p><p>The CCU has the following as members:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_CCU_Membership_Criteria_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_CCU_Membership_Criteria-106">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_CCU_Membership_Members_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_CCU_Membership_Members-107">[42]</a></sup> national laboratories of the Member States of the CGPM charged with establishing national standards;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[bn]</a></sup> relevant intergovernmental organisations and international bodies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[bo]</a></sup>
international commissions or committees;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[bp]</a></sup>
scientific unions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[bq]</a></sup> personal members;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[br]</a></sup>
and, as an <a href="/wiki/Ex_officio_member" title="Ex officio member">ex officio member</a> of all Consultative Committees, the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures#Directors" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">Director of the BIPM</a>.
</p><p>All the decisions and recommendations concerning units are collected in a brochure called <a href="#SI_Brochure"><i>The International System of Units (SI)</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-33"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_Brochure_short_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-SI_Brochure_short-45">[ad]</a></sup> which is published by the BIPM and periodically updated.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Units_and_prefixes"><span class="anchor" id="Coherent_SI_units"></span> Units and prefixes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Units and prefixes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The International System of Units consists of a set of <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">base units</a>, <a href="/wiki/SI_derived_unit" title="SI derived unit">derived units</a>, and a set of decimal-based multipliers that are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_prefix" title="SI prefix">prefixes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 103–106">: 103–106 </span></sup> The units, excluding prefixed units,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[bs]</a></sup> form a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coherent_system_of_units" title="Coherent system of units">coherent system of units</a>, which is based on a system of quantities in such a way that the equations between the numerical values expressed in coherent units have exactly the same form, including numerical factors, as the corresponding equations between the quantities. For example, 1 N = 1 kg × 1 m/s<sup>2</sup> says that <i>one</i> newton is the force required to <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">accelerate</a> a mass of <i>one</i> kilogram at <i>one</i> <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared" title="Metre per second squared">metre per second squared</a>, as related through the principle of coherence to the equation relating the corresponding quantities: <span class="texhtml"><i>F</i> = <i>m</i> × <i>a</i></span>.
</p><p>Derived units apply to derived quantities, which may by definition be expressed in terms of base quantities, and thus are not independent; for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductance" title="Electrical conductance">electrical conductance</a> is the inverse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">electrical resistance</a>, with the consequence that the siemens is the inverse of the ohm, and similarly, the ohm and siemens can be replaced with a ratio of an ampere and a volt, because those quantities bear a defined relationship to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[bt]</a></sup> Other useful derived quantities can be specified in terms of the SI base and derived units that have no named units in the SI, such as acceleration, which is defined in SI units as m/s<sup>2</sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Base_units">Base units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Base units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">SI base unit</a></div>
<p>The SI base units are the building blocks of the system and all the other units are derived from them.
</p>

<td><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle T}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec7200acd984a1d3a3d7dc455e262fbe54f7f6e0" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.636ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>
</td>
<td>The kelvin is defined by setting the fixed numerical value of the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span> to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6977138064900000000♠"></span>1.380<span style="margin-left:.25em;">649</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−23</sup> J⋅K<sup>−1</sup></span>, (J = kg⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup>), given the definition of the kilogram, the metre, and the second.
</td></tr>

<dl><dt>Notes</dt></dl>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Within the context of the SI, the second is the coherent base unit of time, and is used in the definitions of derived units. The name "second" historically arose as being the 2nd-level <a href="/wiki/Sexagesimal" title="Sexagesimal">sexagesimal</a> division (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">60<sup>2</sup></span></span>) of some quantity, the <a href="/wiki/Hour" title="Hour">hour</a> in this case, which the <a href="/wiki/Non-SI_units_mentioned_in_the_SI" title="Non-SI units mentioned in the SI">SI classifies as an "accepted" unit</a> along with its first-level sexagesimal division the <a href="/wiki/Minute" title="Minute">minute</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Symbols for length vary greatly with context. Problems involving intuitive three-dimensional quantities often use <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle l}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle l}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="l" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/829091f745070b9eb97a80244129025440a1cfac" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.693ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle w}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="h" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b26be3e694314bc90c3215047e4a2010c6ee184a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.339ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> for length, distance, and height, respectively. More generally, physicists tend to set up the <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">coordinate system</a> of a given problem so that one axis lies conveniently parallel to the length being measured. Length is then often denoted either by some constant (e.g. <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Despite the prefix "kilo-", the kilogram is the coherent base unit of mass, and is used in the definitions of derived units. Nonetheless, prefixes for the unit of mass are determined as if the gram were the base unit.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When the mole is used, the elementary entities must be specified and may be <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, other particles, or specified groups of such particles.</span>
</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_units">Derived units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Derived units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/SI_derived_unit" title="SI derived unit">SI derived unit</a></div>
<p>The derived units in the SI are formed by powers, products, or quotients of the base units and are potentially unlimited in number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-4"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 103">: 103 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST330_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIST330-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14, 16">: 14, 16 </span></sup> Derived units are associated with derived quantities; for example, <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> is a quantity that is derived from the base quantities of time and length, and thus the SI derived unit is metre per second (symbol m/s). The dimensions of derived units can be expressed in terms of the dimensions of the base units.
</p><p>Combinations of base and derived units may be used to express other derived units. For example, the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a> (N), the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a> (Pa)—and the pascal can be defined as one newton per square metre (N/m<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[47]</a></sup>
</p>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prefixes">Prefixes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Prefixes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metric_prefix" title="Metric prefix">Metric prefix</a></div>
<p>Prefixes are added to unit names to produce multiples and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submultiple" title="Submultiple">submultiples</a> of the original unit. All of these are integer powers of ten, and above a hundred or below a hundredth all are integer powers of a thousand. For example, <i>kilo-</i> denotes a multiple of a thousand and <i>milli-</i> denotes a multiple of a thousandth, so there are one thousand millimetres to the metre and one thousand metres to the kilometre. The prefixes are never combined, so for example a millionth of a metre is a <i>micrometre</i>, not a millimillimetre. Multiples of the kilogram are named as if the gram were the base unit, so a millionth of a kilogram is a <i>milligram</i>, not a microkilogram.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-5"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 122">: 122 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST811_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIST811-125">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup> When prefixes are used to form multiples and submultiples of SI base and derived units, the resulting units are no longer coherent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-6"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup>
</p><p>The BIPM specifies 20 prefixes for the International System of Units (SI):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[bu]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-129">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Prefixes adopted before 1960 already existed before SI. The introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">CGS system</a> was in 1873.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-similar-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-similar_130-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-similar_130-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-similar_130-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-similar_130-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-similar_130-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Part of the beginning of the prefix was modified from the word it was derived from, e.g.: "peta" (prefix) vs "penta" (source word).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The abbreviation mcg is often used in medicine instead of μg to prevent confusion with mg, which can lead to overdoses. A lower case u is often used instead of μ where the latter character is unavailable.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>fermi</i> was introduced earlier with the symbol "fm", which prompted the reinterpretation of the "f" as a prefix to "m", with <i>femto</i> being derived from the Danish word <i>femten</i>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
