<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-SI_units_accepted_for_use_with_SI">Non-SI units accepted for use with SI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Non-SI units accepted for use with SI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-SI_units_accepted_for_use_with_SI" title="Non-SI units accepted for use with SI">Non-SI units accepted for use with SI</a></div>
<p>Many non-SI units continue to be used in the scientific, technical, and commercial literature. Some units are deeply embedded in history and culture, and their use has not been entirely replaced by their SI alternatives. The CIPM recognised and acknowledged such traditions by compiling a list of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-SI_units_accepted_for_use_with_SI" title="Non-SI units accepted for use with SI">non-SI units accepted for use with SI</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-7"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CubeLitre.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="308" data-file-width="385" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/CubeLitre.svg/240px-CubeLitre.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/CubeLitre.svg/360px-CubeLitre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/CubeLitre.svg/480px-CubeLitre.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CubeLitre.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>While not an SI-unit, the litre may be used with SI units. It is equivalent to (10 cm)<sup>3</sup> = (1 dm)<sup>3</sup> = 10<sup>−3</sup> m<sup>3</sup>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some units of time, angle, and legacy non-SI units have a long history of use. Most societies have used the solar day and its non-decimal subdivisions as a basis of time and, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">foot</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">pound</a>, these were the same regardless of where they were being measured. The <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radian</a>, being <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span></span></span> of a revolution, has mathematical advantages but is rarely used for navigation. Further, the units used in navigation around the world are similar. The <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a> were adopted by the CGPM in 1879 and have been retained as units that may be used alongside SI units, having been given unique symbols. The catalogued units are given below:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Non-SI units accepted for use with SI units
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th scope="col"><a href="/wiki/Physical_quantity" title="Physical quantity">Quantity</a>
</th>
<th scope="col">Name
</th>
<th scope="col"><a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">Symbol</a>
</th>
<th scope="col">Value in SI units
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="3"><a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a>
</td>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Minute" title="Minute">minute</a>
</th>

<p>These units are used in combination with SI units in common units such as the kilowatt-hour (1 kW⋅h = 3.6 MJ).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_notions_of_the_metric_units">Common notions of the metric units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Common notions of the metric units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The basic units of the metric system, as originally defined, represented common quantities or relationships in nature. They still do – the modern precisely defined quantities are refinements of definition and methodology, but still with the same magnitudes. In cases where laboratory precision may not be required or available, or where approximations are good enough, the original definitions may suffice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[bv]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>A second is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">60</span></span> of a minute, which is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">60</span></span> of an hour, which is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">24</span></span> of a day, so a second is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004864000000000000♠"></span>86<span style="margin-left:.25em;">400</span></span></span></span> of a day (the use of base 60 dates back to Babylonian times); a second is the time it takes a dense object to freely fall 4.9 metres from rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[bw]</a></sup></li>
<li>The length of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> is close to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7007400000000000000♠"></span>40<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span> m</span> (more precisely <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7007400750142000000♠"></span>40<span style="margin-left:.25em;">075</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">014</span>.2 m</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[52]</a></sup> In fact, the dimensions of our planet were used by the French Academy in the original definition of the metre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[53]</a></sup></li>
<li>The metre is close to the length of <a href="/wiki/Seconds_pendulum" title="Seconds pendulum">a pendulum that has a period of 2 seconds</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[bx]</a></sup> most dining tabletops are about 0.75 metres high;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[54]</a></sup> a very tall human (basketball forward) is about 2 metres tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>The kilogram is the mass of a litre of cold water; a cubic centimetre or millilitre of water has a mass of one gram; a <a href="/wiki/1_euro_coin" title="1 euro coin">1-euro coin</a> weighs 7.5 g;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[56]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Sacagawea_dollar" title="Sacagawea dollar">Sacagawea US 1-dollar coin</a> weighs 8.1 g;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[57]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Fifty_pence_(British_coin)" title="Fifty pence (British coin)">UK 50-pence coin</a> weighs 8.0 g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li>A candela is about the luminous intensity of a moderately bright candle, or 1 candle power; a 60 W tungsten-filament <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulb</a> has a luminous intensity of about 64 candelas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[by]</a></sup></li>
<li>A mole of a substance has a mass that is its <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular mass</a> expressed in units of grams; the mass of a mole of carbon is 12.0 g, and the mass of a mole of table salt is 58.4 g.</li>
<li>Since all gases have the same volume per mole at a given temperature and pressure far from their points of liquefaction and solidification (see <a href="/wiki/Perfect_gas" title="Perfect gas">Perfect gas</a>), and air is about <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> oxygen (molecular mass 32) and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> nitrogen (molecular mass 28), the density of any near-perfect gas relative to air can be obtained to a good approximation by dividing its molecular mass by 29 (because <span class="nowrap"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> × 28 + <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">5</span></span> × 32 = 28.8 ≈ 29</span>). For example, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (molecular mass 28) has almost the same density as air.</li>
<li>A temperature difference of one kelvin is the same as one degree Celsius: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">100</span></span> of the temperature differential between the freezing and boiling points of water at sea level; the absolute temperature in kelvins is the temperature in degrees Celsius plus about 273; <a href="/wiki/Human_body_temperature" title="Human body temperature">human body temperature</a> is about 37 °C or 310 K.</li>
<li>A 60 W incandescent light bulb rated at 120 V (US mains voltage) consumes 0.5 A at this voltage. A 60 W bulb rated at 230 V (European mains voltage) consumes 0.26 A at this voltage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[bz]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lexicographic_conventions">Lexicographic conventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lexicographic conventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/ISO_31-0#Typographic_conventions" title="ISO 31-0">ISO 31-0 § Typographic conventions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_(punctuation)#Unit_symbols_and_numbers" title="Space (punctuation)">Space (punctuation) § Unit symbols and numbers</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unit_names">Unit names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Unit names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the SI Brochure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-34"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">: 148 </span></sup> unit names should be treated as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_noun" title="Common noun">common nouns</a> of the context language. This means that they should be typeset in the same character set as other common nouns (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> in English, <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a> in Russian, etc.), following the usual grammatical and <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthographical</a> rules of the context language. For example, in English and French, even when the unit is named after a person and its symbol begins with a capital letter, the unit name in running text should start with a lowercase letter (e.g., newton, hertz, pascal) and is <a href="/wiki/Capitalization" title="Capitalization">capitalized</a> only at the beginning of a sentence and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Title_capitalization" title="Title capitalization">headings and publication titles</a>. As a nontrivial application of this rule, the SI Brochure notes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-35"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">: 148 </span></sup> that the name of the unit with the symbol <span class="nowrap">°C</span> is correctly spelled as 'degree Celsius': the first letter of the name of the unit, 'd', is in lowercase, while the modifier 'Celsius' is capitalized because it is a proper name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[ca]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-36"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">: 148 </span></sup>
</p><p>The English spelling and even names for certain SI units and metric prefixes depend on the variety of English used. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_English" title="US English">US English</a> uses the spelling <i>deka-</i>, <i>meter</i>, and <i>liter</i>, whilst <a href="/wiki/International_English" title="International English">International English</a> uses <i>deca-</i>, <i>metre</i>, and <i>litre</i>. Additionally, the name of the unit whose symbol is t and which is defined according to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003100000000000000♠"></span>1 t</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>3</sup> kg</span> is 'metric ton' US English but 'tonne' in International English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST330_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-NIST330-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: iii">: iii </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unit_symbols_and_the_values_of_quantities">Unit symbols and the values of quantities <span class="anchor" id="SI_writing_style"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Unit symbols and the values of quantities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Symbols of SI units are intended to be unique and universal, independent of the context language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-8"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 130–135">: 130–135 </span></sup> The SI Brochure has specific rules for writing them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-9"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 130–135">: 130–135 </span></sup> The guideline produced by the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nist_style_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-nist_style-148">[60]</a></sup> clarifies language-specific details for American English that were left unclear by the SI Brochure, but is otherwise identical to the SI Brochure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NISTInterprepation_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-NISTInterprepation-149">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_rules">General rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: General rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>General rules<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-generalrules_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-generalrules-150">[cb]</a></sup> for writing SI units and quantities apply to text that is either handwritten or produced using an automated process:
</p>
<ul><li>The value of a quantity is written as a number followed by a space (representing a multiplication sign) and a unit symbol; e.g., 2.21 kg, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002730000000000000♠"></span>7.3<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>2</sup> m<sup>2</sup></span>, 22 K. This rule explicitly includes the percent sign (%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-10"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 134">: 134 </span></sup> and the symbol for degrees Celsius (°C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-11"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 133">: 133 </span></sup> Exceptions are the symbols for plane angular degrees, minutes, and seconds (°, ′, and ″, respectively), which are placed immediately after the number with no intervening space.</li>
<li>Symbols are mathematical entities, not abbreviations, and as such do not have an appended period/full stop (.), unless the rules of grammar demand one for another reason, such as denoting the end of a sentence.</li>
<li>A prefix is part of the unit, and its symbol is prepended to a unit symbol without a separator (e.g., k in km, M in MPa, G in GHz, μ in μg). Compound prefixes are not allowed. A prefixed unit is atomic in expressions (e.g., km<sup>2</sup> is equivalent to (km)<sup>2</sup>).</li>
<li>Unit symbols are written using roman (upright) type, regardless of the type used in the surrounding text.</li>
<li>Symbols for derived units formed by multiplication are joined with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dot_operator" title="Dot operator">centre dot</a> (⋅) or a non-breaking space; e.g., N⋅m or N m.</li>
<li>Symbols for derived units formed by division are joined with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solidus_(punctuation)" title="Solidus (punctuation)">solidus</a> (/), or given as a negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exponent" title="Exponent">exponent</a>. E.g., the "metre per second" can be written m/s, m s<sup>−1</sup>, m⋅s<sup>−1</sup>, or <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">m</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">s</span></span>. In cases where a solidus is followed by a centre dot (or space), or more than one solidus is present, parentheses must be used to avoid ambiguity; e.g., kg/(m⋅s<sup>2</sup>), kg⋅m<sup>−1</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup>, and (kg/m)/s<sup>2</sup> are acceptable, but kg/m/s<sup>2</sup> and kg/m⋅s<sup>2</sup> are ambiguous and unacceptable.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:981ms2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="85" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/981ms2.png" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:981ms2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the expression of acceleration due to gravity, a space separates the value and the units, both the 'm' and the 's' are lowercase because neither the metre nor the second are named after people, and exponentiation is represented with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superscript" title="Superscript">superscript</a> '2'.</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The first letter of symbols for units derived from the name of a person is written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_case" title="Upper case">upper case</a>; otherwise, they are written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_case" title="Lower case">lower case</a>. E.g., the unit of <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> is named after <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a>, so its symbol is written "Pa", but the symbol for mole is written "mol". Thus, "T" is the symbol for <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">tesla</a>, a measure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_strength" title="Magnetic field strength">magnetic field strength</a>, and "t" the symbol for <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a>, a measure of <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>. Since 1979, the <a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a> may exceptionally be written using either an uppercase "L" or a lowercase "l", a decision prompted by the similarity of the lowercase letter "l" to the numeral "1", especially with certain typefaces or English-style handwriting. The American NIST recommends that within the United States "L" be used rather than "l".</li>
<li>Symbols do not have a plural form, e.g., 25 kg, not 25 kgs.</li>
<li>Uppercase and lowercase prefixes are not interchangeable. E.g., the quantities 1 mW and 1 MW represent two different quantities (milliwatt and megawatt).</li>
<li>The symbol for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decimal_marker" title="Decimal marker">decimal marker</a> is either a <a href="/wiki/Full_stop" title="Full stop">point</a> or <a href="/wiki/Comma" title="Comma">comma</a> on the line. In practice, the decimal point is used in most English-speaking countries and most of Asia, and the comma in most of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and in continental <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_in_Europe" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe">European countries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>Spaces should be used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thousands_separator" title="Thousands separator">thousands separator</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7006100000000000000♠"></span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span>) in contrast to commas or periods (1,000,000 or 1.000.000) to reduce confusion resulting from the variation between these forms in different countries.</li>
<li>Any line-break inside a number, inside a compound unit, or between number and unit should be avoided. Where this is not possible, line breaks should coincide with thousands separators.</li>
<li>Because the value of "billion" and "trillion" <a href="/wiki/Long_and_short_scales" title="Long and short scales">varies between languages</a>, the dimensionless terms "ppb" (parts per <a href="/wiki/Long_and_short_scales" title="Long and short scales">billion</a>) and "ppt" (parts per <a href="/wiki/Long_and_short_scales" title="Long and short scales">trillion</a>) should be avoided. The SI Brochure does not suggest alternatives.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Printing_SI_symbols">Printing SI symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Printing SI symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The rules covering printing of quantities and units are part of ISO 80000-1:2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further rules<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-generalrules_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-generalrules-150">[cb]</a></sup> are specified in respect of production of text using <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing presses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Word_processor" title="Word processor">word processors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriters</a>, and the like.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_System_of_Quantities">International System of Quantities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: International System of Quantities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div style="float:right; padding:1em; margin:0 0 0 1em; width:500px; border:1px solid; background:#faf3ee;"><span class="anchor" id="SI_Brochure"></span>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><big><b>SI Brochure</b></big></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SI_Brochure_Cover.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="212" data-file-width="150" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2b/SI_Brochure_Cover.jpg" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SI_Brochure_Cover.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of brochure <i><a class="external text" href="http://www.bipm.org/en/publications/si-brochure/" rel="nofollow">The International System of Units</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The CGPM publishes a brochure that defines and presents the SI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-12"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup> Its official version is in French, in line with the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-13"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 102">: 102 </span></sup> It leaves some scope for local variations, particularly regarding unit names and terms in different languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[cc]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST330_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-NIST330-3">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The writing and maintenance of the CGPM brochure is carried out by one of the committees of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Committee_for_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Committee for Weights and Measures">International Committee for Weights and Measures</a> (CIPM).
The definitions of the terms "quantity", "unit", "dimension" etc. that are used in the <i>SI Brochure</i> are those given in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_vocabulary_of_metrology" title="International vocabulary of metrology">International vocabulary of metrology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Quantities" title="International System of Quantities">International System of Quantities</a></div>
<p>The quantities and equations that provide the context in which the SI units are defined are now referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Quantities" title="International System of Quantities">International System of Quantities</a></i> (ISQ).
The ISQ is based on the <a href="/wiki/Quantity#Quantity_in_physical_science" title="Quantity">quantities</a> underlying each of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_base_units" title="SI base units">seven base units of the SI</a>. Other quantities, such as <a href="/wiki/Area" title="Area">area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">electrical resistance</a>, are derived from these base quantities by clear non-contradictory equations. The ISQ defines the quantities that are measured with the SI units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[65]</a></sup> The ISQ is formalised, in part, in the international standard <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_80000" title="ISO/IEC 80000">ISO/IEC 80000</a>, which was completed in 2009 with the publication of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_80000-1" title="ISO 80000-1">ISO 80000-1</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[66]</a></sup> and has largely been revised in 2019–2020 with the remainder being under review.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Realisation_of_units">Realisation of units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Realisation of units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Realisation_(metrology)" title="Realisation (metrology)">Realisation (metrology)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="631" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg/170px-Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg/255px-Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg/340px-Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silicon_sphere_for_Avogadro_project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silicon sphere for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avogadro_project" title="Avogadro project">Avogadro project</a> used for measuring the Avogadro constant to a relative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_uncertainty" title="Standard uncertainty">standard uncertainty</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6992200000000000000♠"></span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−8</sup></span> or less, held by <a href="/wiki/Achim_Leistner" title="Achim Leistner">Achim Leistner</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[67]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Metrologists carefully distinguish between the definition of a unit and its realisation. The definition of each base unit of the SI is drawn up so that it is unique and provides a sound theoretical basis on which the most accurate and reproducible measurements can be made. The realisation of the definition of a unit is the procedure by which the definition may be used to establish the value and associated uncertainty of a quantity of the same kind as the unit. A description of the <i>mise en pratique</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Translation_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Translation-158">[cd]</a></sup> of the base units is given in an electronic appendix to the SI Brochure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-14"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 168–169">: 168–169 </span></sup>
</p><p>The published <i>mise en pratique</i> is not the only way in which a base unit can be determined: the SI Brochure states that "any method consistent with the laws of physics could be used to realise any SI unit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-15"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 111">: 111 </span></sup> Various consultative committees of the CIPM decided in 2016 that more than one <i>mise en pratique</i> would be developed for determining the value of each unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[69]</a></sup> These methods include the following:
</p>
<ul><li>At least three separate experiments be carried out yielding values having a relative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_uncertainty" title="Standard uncertainty">standard uncertainty</a> in the determination of the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> of no more than <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6992500000000000000♠"></span>5<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−8</sup></span> and at least one of these values should be better than <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6992200000000000000♠"></span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−8</sup></span>. Both the <a href="/wiki/Kibble_balance" title="Kibble balance">Kibble balance</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avogadro_project" title="Avogadro project">Avogadro project</a> should be included in the experiments and any differences between these be reconciled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[71]</a></sup></li>
<li>The definition of the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> measured with a relative uncertainty of the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> derived from two fundamentally different methods such as acoustic gas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermometry" title="Thermometry">thermometry</a> and dielectric constant gas thermometry be better than one part in <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6994100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>−6</sup></span> and that these values be corroborated by other measurements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[72]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_the_SI">Evolution of the SI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Evolution of the SI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_to_the_SI">Changes to the SI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Changes to the SI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM) has described SI as "the modern form of metric system".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-16"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 95">: 95 </span></sup> Changing technology has led to an evolution of the definitions and standards that has followed two principal strands – changes to SI itself, and clarification of how to use units of measure that are not part of SI but are still nevertheless used on a worldwide basis.
</p><p>Since 1960 the CGPM has made a number of changes to the SI to meet the needs of specific fields, notably chemistry and radiometry. These are mostly additions to the list of named derived units, and include the <i><a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a></i> (symbol mol) for an amount of substance, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a></i> (symbol Pa) for <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Siemens_(unit)" title="Siemens (unit)">siemens</a></i> (symbol S) for electrical conductance, the <i><a href="/wiki/Becquerel" title="Becquerel">becquerel</a></i> (symbol Bq) for "<a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">activity</a> referred to a <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclide</a>", the <i><a href="/wiki/Gray_(unit)" title="Gray (unit)">gray</a></i> (symbol Gy) for ionising radiation, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">sievert</a></i> (symbol Sv) as the unit of dose equivalent radiation, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Katal" title="Katal">katal</a></i> (symbol kat) for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalytic_activity" title="Catalytic activity">catalytic activity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-17"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 156">: 156 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-18"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 156">: 156 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-19"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 158">: 158 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-20"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 159">: 159 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-21"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 165">: 165 </span></sup>
</p><p>The range of defined prefixes pico- (10<sup>−12</sup>) to tera- (10<sup>12</sup>) was extended to 10<sup>−24</sup> to 10<sup>24</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-22"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 152">: 152 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-23"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 158">: 158 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-24"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 164">: 164 </span></sup>
</p><p>The 1960 definition of the standard metre in terms of wavelengths of a specific emission of the krypton-86 atom was replaced in 1983 with the distance that light travels in vacuum in exactly <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span></span></span></span> second, so that the speed of light is now an exactly specified constant of nature.
</p><p>A few changes to notation conventions have also been made to alleviate lexicographic ambiguities. An analysis under the aegis of <a href="/wiki/CSIRO" title="CSIRO">CSIRO</a>, published in 2009 by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, has pointed out the opportunities to finish the realisation of that goal, to the point of universal zero-ambiguity machine readability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foster_2009_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foster_2009-165">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2019_redefinitions">2019 redefinitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: 2019 redefinitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="625" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg/220px-Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg/330px-Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg/440px-Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Unit_relations_in_the_new_SI.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reverse dependencies of the SI base units on seven <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">physical constants</a>, which are assigned exact numerical values in the 2019 redefinition. Unlike in the previous definitions, the base units are all derived exclusively from constants of nature. Here, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a\rightarrow b}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>a</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<mi>b</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a\rightarrow b}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a\rightarrow b" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/390afdb0d8e933dbc4eee8022d8c91b2015f0522" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.841ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> means that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle a}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>a</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle a}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="a" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.23ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is used to define <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle b}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>b</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle b}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="b" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f11423fbb2e967f986e36804a8ae4271734917c3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.998ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2019_redefinition_of_the_SI_base_units" title="2019 redefinition of the SI base units">2019 redefinition of the SI base units</a></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metre" title="History of the metre">metre was redefined</a> in 1960, the <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_of_the_Kilogram" title="International Prototype of the Kilogram">International Prototype of the Kilogram</a> (IPK) was the only physical artefact upon which base units (directly the kilogram and indirectly the ampere, mole and candela) depended for their definition, making these units subject to periodic comparisons of national standard kilograms with the IPK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPL_kg_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPL_kg-166">[75]</a></sup> During the 2nd and 3rd Periodic Verification of National Prototypes of the Kilogram, a significant divergence had occurred between the mass of the IPK and all of its official copies stored around the world: the copies had all noticeably increased in mass with respect to the IPK. During <i>extraordinary verifications</i> carried out in 2014 preparatory to redefinition of metric standards, continuing divergence was not confirmed. Nonetheless, the residual and irreducible instability of a physical IPK undermined the reliability of the entire metric system to precision measurement from small (atomic) to large (astrophysical) scales.
</p><p>A proposal was made that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>In addition to the speed of light, four constants of nature – the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a> – be defined to have exact values</li>
<li>The International Prototype of the Kilogram be retired</li>
<li>The current definitions of the kilogram, ampere, kelvin, and mole be revised</li>
<li>The wording of base unit definitions should change emphasis from explicit unit to explicit constant definitions.</li></ul>
<p>The new definitions were adopted at the 26th CGPM on 16 November 2018, and came into effect on 20 May 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[77]</a></sup> The change was adopted by the European Union through Directive (EU) 2019/1258.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg/170px-Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg/255px-Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg/340px-Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alter_Grenzstein_Pontebba_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boundary_marker" title="Boundary marker">Stone marking</a> the <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a>/Italian border at <a href="/wiki/Pontebba" title="Pontebba">Pontebba</a> displaying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myriametre" title="Myriametre">myriametres</a>, a unit of 10 km used in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> in the 19th century (but since <a href="/wiki/Deprecation" title="Deprecation">deprecated</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa1842_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Europa1842-170">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metric_system" title="History of the metric system">History of the metric system</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_improvisation_of_units">The improvisation of units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: The improvisation of units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The units and unit magnitudes of the metric system which became the SI were improvised piecemeal from everyday physical quantities starting in the mid-18th century. Only later were they moulded into an orthogonal coherent decimal system of measurement.
</p><p>The degree centigrade as a unit of temperature resulted from the scale devised by Swedish astronomer <a href="/wiki/Anders_Celsius" title="Anders Celsius">Anders Celsius</a> in 1742. His scale counter-intuitively designated 100 as the freezing point of water and 0 as the boiling point. Independently, in 1743, the French physicist <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Christin" title="Jean-Pierre Christin">Jean-Pierre Christin</a> described a scale with 0 as the freezing point of water and 100 the boiling point. The scale became known as the centi-grade, or 100 gradations of temperature, scale.
</p><p>The metric system was developed from 1791 onwards by a committee of the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a>, commissioned to create a unified and rational system of measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[80]</a></sup> The group, which included preeminent French men of science,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-172">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 89">: 89 </span></sup> used the same principles for relating length, volume, and mass that had been proposed by the English clergyman <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkins" title="John Wilkins">John Wilkins</a> in 1668<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[83]</a></sup> and the concept of using the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(geography)" title="Meridian (geography)">meridian</a> as the basis of the definition of length, originally proposed in 1670 by the French abbot <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Mouton" title="Gabriel Mouton">Mouton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mouton_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mouton-176">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="917" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg/170px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg/255px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg/340px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>In March 1791, the Assembly adopted the committee's proposed principles for the new decimal system of measure including the metre defined to be 1/10,000,000 of the length of the quadrant of Earth's meridian passing through Paris, and authorised a survey to precisely establish the length of the meridian. In July 1792, the committee proposed the names <i><a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">metre</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Are_(unit)" title="Are (unit)">are</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Litre" title="Litre">litre</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Grave_(unit)" title="Grave (unit)">grave</a></i> for the units of length, area, capacity, and mass, respectively. The committee also proposed that multiples and submultiples of these units were to be denoted by decimal-based prefixes such as <i>centi</i> for a hundredth and <i>kilo</i> for a thousand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tavernor_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tavernor-177">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 82">: 82 </span></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:301px;max-width:301px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:142px;max-width:142px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Thomson</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:155px;max-width:155px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png"><img alt="James Clerk Maxwell" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="835" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png/153px-PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png/230px-PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png/306px-PSM_V78_D529_James_Clerk_Maxwell.png 2x" width="153"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center"><a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">Maxwell</a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">William Thomson (Lord Kelvin) and James Clerk Maxwell played a prominent role in the development of the principle of coherence and in the naming of many units of measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIHistory_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIHistory-178">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LordKelvin_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-LordKelvin-179">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-special_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-special-180">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPMCentenary_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPMCentenary-181">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell2_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2-182">[91]</a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Later, during the process of adoption of the metric system, the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gramme</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogramme</a></i>, replaced the former provincial terms <i>gravet</i> (1/1000 <i>grave</i>) and <i>grave</i>. In June 1799, based on the results of the meridian survey, the standard <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A8tre_des_Archives" title="Mètre des Archives">mètre des Archives</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilogramme_des_Archives" title="Kilogramme des Archives">kilogramme des Archives</a></i> were deposited in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archives_nationales_(France)" title="Archives nationales (France)">French National Archives</a>. Subsequently, that year, the metric system was adopted by law in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[92]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[93]</a></sup> The French system was short-lived due to its unpopularity. Napoleon ridiculed it, and in 1812, introduced a replacement system, the <i>mesures usuelles</i> or "customary measures" which restored many of the old units, but redefined in terms of the metric system.
</p><p>During the first half of the 19th century there was little consistency in the choice of preferred multiples of the base units: typically the myriametre (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004100000000000000♠"></span>10<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span></span> metres) was in widespread use in both France and parts of Germany, while the kilogram (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003100000000000000♠"></span>1000</span> grams) rather than the myriagram was used for mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Europa1842_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Europa1842-170">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1832, the German <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">Wilhelm Weber</a>, implicitly defined the second as a base unit when he quoted the Earth's magnetic field in terms of millimetres, grams, and seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIHistory_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIHistory-178">[87]</a></sup> Prior to this, the strength of the Earth's magnetic field had only been described in <a href="/wiki/Relative_change_and_difference" title="Relative change and difference">relative terms</a>. The technique used by Gauss was to equate the <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a> induced on a suspended magnet of known mass by the Earth's magnetic field with the torque induced on an equivalent system under gravity. The resultant calculations enabled him to assign dimensions based on mass, length and time to the magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[ce]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>A candlepower as a unit of illuminance was originally defined by an 1860 English law as the light produced by a pure <a href="/wiki/Spermaceti" title="Spermaceti">spermaceti</a> candle weighing <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020198016" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">6</span></span> pound (76 grams) and burning at a specified rate. Spermaceti, a waxy substance found in the heads of sperm whales, was once used to make high-quality candles. At this time the French standard of light was based upon the illumination from a <a href="/wiki/Carcel_lamp" title="Carcel lamp">Carcel oil lamp</a>. The unit was defined as that illumination emanating from a lamp burning pure <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed_oil" title="Rapeseed oil">rapeseed oil</a> at a defined rate. It was accepted that ten standard candles were about equal to one Carcel lamp.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metre_Convention">Metre Convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Metre Convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a></div>
<p>A French-inspired initiative for international cooperation in <a href="/wiki/Metrology" title="Metrology">metrology</a> led to the signing in 1875 of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>, also called Treaty of the Metre, by 17 nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[cf]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alder_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alder-172">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 353–354">: 353–354 </span></sup> Initially the convention only covered standards for the metre and the kilogram. In 1921, the Metre Convention was extended to include all physical units, including the ampere and others thereby enabling the CGPM to address inconsistencies in the way that the metric system had been used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LordKelvin_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-LordKelvin-179">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-25"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 96">: 96 </span></sup>
</p><p>A set of 30 prototypes of the metre and 40 prototypes of the kilogram,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[cg]</a></sup> in each case made of a 90% <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>-10% <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a> alloy, were manufactured by <i>British metallurgy specialty firm</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2021)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> and accepted by the CGPM in 1889. One of each was selected at random to become the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_prototype_metre" title="International prototype metre">International prototype metre</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_prototype_kilogram" title="International prototype kilogram">International prototype kilogram</a> that replaced the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A8tre_des_Archives" title="Mètre des Archives">mètre des Archives</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilogramme_des_Archives" title="Kilogramme des Archives">kilogramme des Archives</a></i> respectively. Each member state was entitled to one of each of the remaining prototypes to serve as the national prototype for that country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-189">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The treaty also established a number of international organisations to oversee the keeping of international standards of measurement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[ch]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_CGS_and_MKS_systems">The CGS and MKS systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: The CGS and MKS systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGS_system_of_units" title="CGS system of units">CGS system of units</a> and <a href="/wiki/MKS_system_of_units" title="MKS system of units">MKS system of units</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_National_Length_Meter.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/US_National_Length_Meter.JPG/220px-US_National_Length_Meter.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/US_National_Length_Meter.JPG/330px-US_National_Length_Meter.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/US_National_Length_Meter.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_National_Length_Meter.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Closeup of the National Prototype Metre, serial number 27, allocated to the United States</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1860s, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">William Thomson</a> (later Lord Kelvin) and others working under the auspices of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="British Association for the Advancement of Science">British Association for the Advancement of Science</a>, built on Gauss's work and formalised the concept of a coherent system of units with base units and derived units christened the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">centimetre–gram–second system of units</a> in 1874. The principle of coherence was successfully used to define a number of units of measure based on the CGS, including the <a href="/wiki/Erg" title="Erg">erg</a> for <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dyne" title="Dyne">dyne</a> for <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Barye" title="Barye">barye</a> for <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Poise_(unit)" title="Poise (unit)">poise</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamic_viscosity" title="Dynamic viscosity">dynamic viscosity</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stokes_(unit)" title="Stokes (unit)">stokes</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinematic_viscosity" title="Kinematic viscosity">kinematic viscosity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPMCentenary_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-BIPMCentenary-181">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1879, the CIPM published recommendations for writing the symbols for length, area, volume and mass, but it was outside its domain to publish recommendations for other quantities. Beginning in about 1900, physicists who had been using the symbol "μ" (mu) for "micrometre" or "micron", "λ" (lambda) for "microlitre", and "γ" (gamma) for "microgram" started to use the symbols "μm", "μL" and "μg".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the close of the 19th century three different systems of units of measure existed for electrical measurements: a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGS-based_system_for_electrostatic_units" title="CGS-based system for electrostatic units">CGS-based system for electrostatic units</a>, also known as the Gaussian or ESU system, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGS-based_system_for_electromechanical_units" title="CGS-based system for electromechanical units">CGS-based system for electromechanical units</a> (EMU) and an International system based on units defined by the Metre Convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[98]</a></sup> for electrical distribution systems. 
Attempts to resolve the electrical units in terms of length, mass, and time using <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimensional analysis</a> was beset with difficulties—the dimensions depended on whether one used the ESU or EMU systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell2_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2-182">[91]</a></sup> This anomaly was resolved in 1901 when <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Giorgi" title="Giovanni Giorgi">Giovanni Giorgi</a> published a paper in which he advocated using a fourth base unit alongside the existing three base units. The fourth unit could be chosen to be <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">electrical resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IECGiorgi_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-IECGiorgi-194">[99]</a></sup> Electric current with named unit 'ampere' was chosen as the base unit, and the other electrical quantities derived from it according to the laws of physics. This became the foundation of the MKS system of units.
</p><p>In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, a number of non-coherent units of measure based on the gram/kilogram, centimetre/metre, and second, such as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pferdest%C3%A4rke" title="Pferdestärke">Pferdestärke</a></i> (metric horsepower) for <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[ci]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Darcy_(unit)" title="Darcy (unit)">darcy</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permeability_(earth_sciences)" title="Permeability (earth sciences)">permeability</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[101]</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/Torr#Manometric_units_of_pressure" title="Torr">millimetres of mercury</a>" for <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">barometric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> were developed or propagated, some of which incorporated <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">standard gravity</a> in their definitions.
</p><p>At the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, a number of different systems of measurement were in use throughout the world. Some of these systems were metric system variations; others were based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systems_of_measurement" title="Systems of measurement">customary systems</a> of measure, like the US customary system and British Imperial system.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Practical_system_of_units">The <i>Practical system of units</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: The Practical system of units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1948, the 9th CGPM commissioned a study to assess the measurement needs of the scientific, technical, and educational communities and "to make recommendations for a single practical system of units of measurement, suitable for adoption by all countries adhering to the Metre Convention".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[102]</a></sup> This working document was <i>Practical system of units of measurement</i>. Based on this study, the 10th CGPM in 1954 defined an international system derived from six base units including units of temperature and optical radiation in addition to those for the MKS system mass, length, and time units and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Giorgi" title="Giovanni Giorgi">Giorgi</a>'s current unit. Six base units were recommended: the metre, kilogram, second, ampere, degree Kelvin, and candela.
</p><p>The 9th CGPM also approved the first formal recommendation for the writing of symbols in the metric system when the basis of the rules as they are now known was laid down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[103]</a></sup> These rules were subsequently extended and now cover unit symbols and names, prefix symbols and names, how quantity symbols should be written and used, and how the values of quantities should be expressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-26"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 104, 130">: 104, 130 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_the_SI">Birth of the SI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Birth of the SI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1960, the 11th CGPM synthesised the results of the 12-year study into a set of 16 resolutions. The system was named the <i>International System of Units</i>, abbreviated SI from the French name, <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Le Système International d'Unités</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure_97-27"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure-97">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 110">: 110 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_definitions">Historical definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Historical definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">Maxwell</a> first introduced the concept of a coherent system, he identified three quantities that could be used as base units: mass, length, and time. <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Giorgi" title="Giovanni Giorgi">Giorgi</a> later identified the need for an electrical base unit, for which the unit of electric current was chosen for SI. Another three base units (for temperature, amount of substance, and luminous intensity) were added later.
</p><p>The early metric systems defined a unit of weight as a base unit, while the SI defines an analogous unit of mass. In everyday use, these are mostly interchangeable, but in scientific contexts the difference matters. Mass, strictly the inertial mass, represents a quantity of matter. It relates the acceleration of a body to the applied force via <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's law</a>, <span class="nowrap"><i>F</i> = <i>m</i> × <i>a</i></span>: force equals mass times acceleration. A force of 1 N (newton) applied to a mass of 1 kg will accelerate it at 1 m/s<sup>2</sup>. This is true whether the object is floating in space or in a gravity field e.g. at the Earth's surface. Weight is the force exerted on a body by a gravitational field, and hence its weight depends on the strength of the gravitational field. Weight of a 1 kg mass at the Earth's surface is <span class="nowrap"><i>m</i> × <i>g</i></span>; mass times the acceleration due to gravity, which is 9.81 newtons at the Earth's surface and is about 3.5 newtons at the surface of Mars. Since the acceleration due to gravity is local and varies by location and altitude on the Earth, weight is unsuitable for precision measurements of a property of a body, and this makes a unit of weight unsuitable as a base unit.
</p>

<li><b>Current</b> (1967): The duration of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7009919263177000000♠"></span>9<span style="margin-left:.25em;">192</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">631</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">770</span></span> periods of the radiation corresponding to the transition between the two <a href="/wiki/Hyperfine_structure" title="Hyperfine structure">hyperfine</a> levels of the <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesium-133" title="Caesium-133">caesium-133</a> atom.</li></ul>

<li><b>Interim</b> (1889): The Prototype of the metre chosen by the CIPM, at the temperature of melting ice, represents the metric unit of length.</li>
<li><b>Interim</b> (1960): <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7006165076373000000♠"></span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">650</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">763</span>.73</span> <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">radiation</a> corresponding to the transition between the 2p<sup>10</sup> and 5d<sup>5</sup> quantum levels of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krypton-86" title="Krypton-86">krypton-86</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a>.</li>
<li><b>Current</b> (1983): The distance travelled by light in vacuum in <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span></span></span></span> second.</li></ul>
