<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a>
</th>
<td>
<ul><li><b>Prior</b> (1793): The <b><a href="/wiki/Grave_(unit)" title="Grave (unit)">grave</a></b> was defined as being the mass (then called <i>weight</i>) of one litre of pure water at its freezing point.<sup>FG</sup></li>
<li><b>Interim</b> (1889): The mass of a small squat cylinder of ≈47 cubic centimetres of platinum-iridium alloy kept in the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Burueau of Weights and Measures</a> (BIPM), <a href="/wiki/Pavillon_de_Breteuil" title="Pavillon de Breteuil">Pavillon de Breteuil</a>, France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[cj]</a></sup> Also, in practice, any of numerous official replicas of it.</li>
<li><b>Current</b> (2019): The kilogram is defined by setting the <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">Planck constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span> exactly to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6966662607015000000♠"></span>6.626<span style="margin-left:.25em;">070</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">15</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−34</sup> J⋅s</span> (<span class="nowrap">J = kg⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup></span>), given the definitions of the metre and the second.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_2018-11_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIST_2018-11-95">[33]</a></sup> Then the formula would be <span class="nowrap">kg = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6966662607015000000♠"></span>6.626<span style="margin-left:.25em;">070</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">15</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−34</sup></span>⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup></span></span></span></li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a>
</th>
<td>
<ul><li><b>Prior</b> (1881): A tenth of the electromagnetic CGS unit of current. The [CGS] electromagnetic unit of current is that current, flowing in an arc 1 cm long of a circle 1 cm in radius, that creates a field of one <a href="/wiki/Oersted" title="Oersted">oersted</a> at the centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[105]</a></sup> <sup>IEC</sup></li>
<li><b>Interim</b> (1946): The constant current which, if maintained in two straight parallel conductors of infinite length, of negligible circular cross-section, and placed 1 m apart in vacuum, would produce between these conductors a <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> equal to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6993200000000000000♠"></span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−7</sup></span> <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newtons</a> per metre of length.</li>
<li><b>Current</b> (2019): The flow of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6981160217663400000♠"></span>1.602<span style="margin-left:.25em;">176</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">634</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−19</sup></span></span></span> times the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">elementary charge</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span> per second.</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>
</th>
<td>
<ul><li><b>Prior</b> (1743): The <b>centigrade scale</b> is obtained by assigning 0 °C to the <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">freezing</a> point of water and 100 °C to the <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> of water.</li>
<li><b>Interim</b> (1954): The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_point_of_water" title="Triple point of water">triple point of water</a> (0.01 °C) defined to be exactly 273.16 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[n 2]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Previous</b> (1967): <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">273.16</span></span> of the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a> of the triple point of water.</li>
<li><b>Current</b> (2019): The kelvin is defined by setting the fixed numerical value of the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span> to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6977138064900000000♠"></span>1.380<span style="margin-left:.25em;">649</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−23</sup> J⋅K<sup>−1</sup></span>, (J = kg⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup>), given the definition of the kilogram, the metre, and the second.</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a>
</th>
<td>
<ul><li><b>Prior</b> (1900): A stoichiometric quantity which is the equivalent mass in grams of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avogadro%27s_number" title="Avogadro's number">Avogadro's number</a> of molecules of a substance.<sup>ICAW</sup></li>
<li><b>Interim</b> (1967): The amount of substance of a system which contains as many elementary entities as there are atoms in 0.012 kilogram of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a>.</li>
<li><b>Current</b> (2019): The amount of substance of exactly <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7023602214076000000♠"></span>6.022<span style="margin-left:.25em;">140</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">76</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>23</sup></span> elementary entities. This number is the fixed numerical value of the <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro constant</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>N</i></span><sub>A</sub>, when expressed in the unit mol<sup>−1</sup> and is called the Avogadro number.</li></ul>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Candela" title="Candela">candela</a>
</th>
<td>
<ul><li><b>Prior</b> (1946): The value of the new candle (early name for the candela) is such that the brightness of the full radiator at the temperature of solidification of <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> is 60 new candles per square centimetre.</li>
<li><b>Current</b> (1979): The luminous intensity, in a given direction, of a source that emits monochromatic radiation of frequency <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7014540000000000000♠"></span>5.4<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>14</sup></span> hertz and that has a radiant intensity in that direction of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">683</span></span> watt per <a href="/wiki/Steradian" title="Steradian">steradian</a>.</li></ul>
<dl><dd>Note: both old and new definitions are approximately the luminous intensity of a <a href="/wiki/Spermaceti" title="Spermaceti">spermaceti</a> candle burning modestly bright, in the late 19th century called a "candlepower" or a "candle".</dd></dl>
</td></tr>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Metric_units_that_are_not_recognised_by_the_SI">Metric units that are not recognised by the SI</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Metric units that are not recognised by the SI">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_units" title="Metric units">Metric units</a></div>
<p>Although the term <i>metric system</i> is often used as an informal alternative name for the International System of Units,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetricSystemAsNameForSI_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-MetricSystemAsNameForSI-205">[106]</a></sup> other metric systems exist, some of which were in widespread use in the past or are even still used in particular areas. There are also individual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_units" title="Metric units">metric units</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Sverdrup" title="Sverdrup">sverdrup</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Darcy_(unit)" title="Darcy (unit)">darcy</a> that exist outside of any system of units. Most of the units of the other metric systems are not recognised by the SI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[ck]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[cn]</a></sup>
</p><p>Here are some examples. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second" title="Centimetre–gram–second">centimetre–gram–second (CGS)</a> system was the dominant metric system in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">physical sciences</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_engineering" title="Electrical engineering">electrical engineering</a> from the 1860s until at least the 1960s, and is still in use in some fields. It includes such SI-unrecognised units as the <a href="/wiki/Gal_(unit)" title="Gal (unit)">gal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dyne" title="Dyne">dyne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erg" title="Erg">erg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barye" title="Barye">barye</a>, etc. in its <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanical</a> sector, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Poise_(unit)" title="Poise (unit)">poise</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centistokes" title="Centistokes">stokes</a> in fluid dynamics. When it comes to the units for quantities in electricity and magnetism, there are several versions of the CGS system. Two of these are obsolete: the <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units#Electrostatic_units_(ESU)" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">CGS electrostatic</a> ('CGS-ESU', with the SI-unrecognised units of <a href="/wiki/Statcoulomb" title="Statcoulomb">statcoulomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statvolt" title="Statvolt">statvolt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centimetre%E2%80%93gram%E2%80%93second_system_of_units#Electrostatic_units_(ESU)" title="Centimetre–gram–second system of units">statampere</a>, etc.) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_units" title="Electromagnetic units">CGS electromagnetic</a> system ('CGS-EMU', with <a href="/wiki/Abampere" title="Abampere">abampere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abcoulomb" title="Abcoulomb">abcoulomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oersted" title="Oersted">oersted</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_(unit)" title="Maxwell (unit)">maxwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abhenry" title="Abhenry">abhenry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_(unit)" title="Gilbert (unit)">gilbert</a>, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corresponding_not_equal_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corresponding_not_equal-213">[co]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stat_and_abstat_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-stat_and_abstat-217">[cq]</a></sup> A 'blend' of these two systems is still popular and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_units" title="Gaussian units">Gaussian system</a> (which includes the <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> as a special name for the CGS-EMU unit maxwell per square centimetre).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaussian_as_blend_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaussian_as_blend-218">[cr]</a></sup>
</p><p>In engineering (other than electrical engineering), there was formerly a long tradition of using the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_metric_system" title="Gravitational metric system">gravitational metric system</a>, whose SI-unrecognised units include the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram-force" title="Kilogram-force">kilogram-force</a> (kilopond), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technical_atmosphere" title="Technical atmosphere">technical atmosphere</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_horsepower" title="Metric horsepower">metric horsepower</a>, etc. The <a href="/wiki/Metre%E2%80%93tonne%E2%80%93second_system_of_units" title="Metre–tonne–second system of units">metre–tonne–second (mts)</a> system, used in the Soviet Union from 1933 to 1955, had such SI-unrecognised units as the <a href="/wiki/Sth%C3%A8ne" title="Sthène">sthène</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pi%C3%A8ze" title="Pièze">pièze</a>, etc. Other groups of SI-unrecognised metric units are the various legacy and CGS units related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionising_radiation" title="Ionising radiation">ionising radiation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rutherford_(unit)" title="Rutherford (unit)">rutherford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curie_(unit)" title="Curie (unit)">curie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roentgen_(unit)" title="Roentgen (unit)">roentgen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rad_(unit)" title="Rad (unit)">rad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roentgen_equivalent_man" title="Roentgen equivalent man">rem</a>, etc.), <a href="/wiki/Radiometry" title="Radiometry">radiometry</a> (<a href="/wiki/Langley_(unit)" title="Langley (unit)">langley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jansky" title="Jansky">jansky</a>), <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(optics)" title="Photometry (optics)">photometry</a> (<a href="/wiki/Phot" title="Phot">phot</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nox_(unit)" title="Nox (unit)">nox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stilb_(unit)" title="Stilb (unit)">stilb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Candela_per_square_metre" title="Candela per square metre">nit</a>, metre-candle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trotter_Illumination_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trotter_Illumination-219">[112]</a></sup><sup>:<a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=zlBDAAAAIAAJ&amp;newbks=0&amp;pg=PA17#v=onepage&amp;q=%22metre-candle%22" rel="nofollow">17</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lambert_(unit)" title="Lambert (unit)">lambert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apostilb" title="Apostilb">apostilb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skot_(unit)" title="Skot (unit)">skot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bril_(unit)" title="Bril (unit)">brill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troland" title="Troland">troland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumen_second" title="Lumen second">talbot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Candlepower" title="Candlepower">candlepower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Candela#History" title="Candela">candle</a>), thermodynamics (<a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calorie</a>), and spectroscopy (<a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">reciprocal centimetre</a>).
</p><p>Some other SI-unrecognised metric units that don't fit into any of the already mentioned categories include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Are_(unit)" title="Are (unit)">are</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">barn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fermi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gradian" title="Gradian">gradian (gon, grad, or grade)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carat_(mass)" title="Carat (mass)">metric carat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micrometre#SI_standardization" title="Micrometre">micron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millimetre_of_mercury" title="Millimetre of mercury">millimetre of mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Torr" title="Torr">torr</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centimetre_of_water" title="Centimetre of water">millimetre (or centimetre, or metre) of water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanometre#History" title="Nanometre">millimicron</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mho" title="Mho">mho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stere" title="Stere">stere</a>, <a href="/wiki/X_unit" title="X unit">x unit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamma#Mathematics_and_science" title="Gamma"><span class="texhtml">γ</span> (unit of mass)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamma#Mathematics_and_science" title="Gamma"><span class="texhtml">γ</span> (unit of magnetic flux density)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lambda_(unit)" title="Lambda (unit)"><span class="texhtml">λ</span> (unit of volume)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEEE/ASTM_SI_10-2016_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEEE/ASTM_SI_10-2016-220">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title="Page / location: 20–21
Quotation : &quot;Table 8 lists units and names that shall not be used and gives SI equivalents that shall be used in their place. These are examples of several metric and related units other than those of SI that have been defined over the years. table has an entry for 'G, g (as a unit)', given as 9.80665 m/s.&quot;">20–21</span> </sup> In some cases, the SI-unrecognised metric units have equivalent SI units formed by combining a <a href="/wiki/Metric_prefix" title="Metric prefix">metric prefix</a> with a coherent SI unit. For example, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 <span class="texhtml">γ</span> (unit of magnetic flux density)</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6991100000000000000♠"></span>1 nT</span>, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 Gal</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 cm⋅s<sup>−2</sup></span>, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 barye</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 <a href="/wiki/Deci-" title="Deci-">deci</a><a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">pascal</a></span>, etc. (a related group are the correspondences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corresponding_not_equal_213-1"><a href="#cite_note-Corresponding_not_equal-213">[co]</a></sup> such as <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 abampere</span> ≘ <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 <a href="/wiki/Deca-" title="Deca-">deca</a><a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">ampere</a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 abhenry</span> ≘ <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 <a href="/wiki/Nano-" title="Nano-">nano</a><a href="/wiki/Henry_(unit)" title="Henry (unit)">henry</a></span>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[cs]</a></sup>). Sometimes, it is not even a matter of a metric prefix: the SI-nonrecognised unit may be exactly the same as an SI coherent unit, except for the fact that the SI does not recognise the special name and symbol. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Candela_per_square_metre" title="Candela per square metre">nit</a> is just an SI-unrecognised name for the SI unit <a href="/wiki/Candela_per_square_metre" title="Candela per square metre">candela per square metre</a> and the talbot is an SI-unrecognised name for the SI unit <a href="/wiki/Lumen_second" title="Lumen second">lumen second</a>. Frequently, a non-SI metric unit is related to an SI unit through a power-of-ten factor, but not one that has a metric prefix, e.g., <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6995100000000000000♠"></span>1 dyn</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6995100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>−5</sup> newton</span>, The <a href="/wiki/Angstrom" title="Angstrom">angstrom</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6990100000000000000♠"></span>1 Å</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6990100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>−10</sup> m</span>), still used in various fields, etc. (and correspondences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corresponding_not_equal_213-2"><a href="#cite_note-Corresponding_not_equal-213">[co]</a></sup> like <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 gauss</span> ≘ <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6996100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>−4</sup> <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">tesla</a></span>). Finally, there are metric units whose conversion factors to SI units are not powers of ten, e.g., <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 calorie</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000418400000000000♠"></span>4.184 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 kilogram-force</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000980665000000000♠"></span>9.806<span style="margin-left:.25em;">650</span> <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newtons</a></span>. Some SI-unrecognised metric units are still frequently used, e.g., the calorie (in nutrition), the rem (in the US), the jansky (in <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio astronomy</a>), the gauss (in industry) and the CGS-Gaussian units<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaussian_as_blend_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gaussian_as_blend-218">[cr]</a></sup> more generally (in some subfields of physics), the metric horsepower (for engine power, in most of the non-English speaking world), the kilogram-force (for rocket engine thrust, in China and sometimes in Europe), etc. Others are now rarely used, such as the sthène and the rutherford.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">'SI' is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Initialism" title="Initialism">initialism</a> of <i>Système international,</i> which is an abbreviated form of its full <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> name <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Système international d'unités</i></span>,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 165">: 165 </span></sup> which literally means 'International System of Units'. By Resolution 12 of the 11th <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">CGPM</a> (1960), <i>the international abbreviation of the name of the system is: SI.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 165">: 165 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When we say 'SI system', we are basically saying the word 'system' twice: 'International System system' (note that 'SI' stands for the French name <i>Système international,</i> which literally means 'International System'). This is a type of <a href="/wiki/Redundancy_(linguistics)" title="Redundancy (linguistics)">linguistic redundancy</a> called <a href="/wiki/Pleonasm#Acronyms_and_initialisms" title="Pleonasm">pleonasm</a>. Some examples of such pleonastic usage include the list of 'alternate titles' in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_Britannica" title="Encyclopedia Britannica">Encyclopedia Britannica</a></i> article on the SI,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_SI_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_SI-4">[3]</a></sup> the last paragraph in an editorial in the journal <a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)"><i>Nature</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SI_units_need_reform_to_avoid_confusion_Nature_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-SI_units_need_reform_to_avoid_confusion_Nature-5">[4]</a></sup> and the footnote 1 to Table 5 in the style manual of the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Web_IAU_Style_Manual_SI_Units_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Web_IAU_Style_Manual_SI_Units-6">[5]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a decimal system, different units for a given kind of physical quantity are related by factors of 10, so that, within such a system, unit conversions involve the simple process of moving the decimal point to the right or to the left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAndMetricSystem_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAndMetricSystem-10">[8]</a></sup> So instead of relations like 1 mile =  <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003176000000000000♠"></span>1760</span> yards, as we have in <a href="/wiki/Imperial_and_US_customary_measurement_systems" title="Imperial and US customary measurement systems">imperial and US customary measurement systems</a> (which are not decimal), in the SI (which is decimal) we instead have 1 kilometre = 1000 metres. Here the kilometre is comparable in size to the mile (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003100000000000000♠"></span>1 km</span> ≈ <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002965606400000000♠"></span>0.6 mi</span>) and the metre to the yard (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 m</span> ≈ <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100584000000000♠"></span>1.1 yd</span>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Or one of its decimal multiples or submultiples, like the centimetre.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Or one of its decimal multiples or submultiples, like the gram.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Or one of its decimal multiples or submultiples, like the gram-force.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A metric system of units is any system of weights and measures that is decimal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[c]</a></sup> and based on the metre<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[d]</a></sup> as the unit of length and either the kilogram<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[e]</a></sup> as the unit of mass or the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram-force" title="Kilogram-force">kilogram-force</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[f]</a></sup> as the unit of force.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As of 19 January 2021.<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Economies_of_the_General_Conference-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Economies_of_the_General_Conference_20-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Economies_of_the_General_Conference_20-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The latter group includes <a href="/wiki/Economic_union" title="Economic union">economic unions</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Community" title="Caribbean Community">Caribbean Community</a> (CARICOM).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-CGPM_briefly_explained-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CGPM_briefly_explained_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is an international organization with<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[h]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures#Member_States" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">63 member states</a> and 39 Associate States and Economies of the General Conference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economies_of_the_General_Conference_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economies_of_the_General_Conference-20">[i]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_CGPM_Members_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIPM_CGPM_Members-21">[12]</a></sup> It was established in 1875 under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FR_Interpretation_of_the_SI_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FR_Interpretation_of_the_SI-18">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metre_Convention_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metre_Convention-22">[13]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-French_CGPM-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-French_CGPM_24-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_CGPM_24-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_CGPM_24-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">From <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i>Conférence générale des poids et mesures.</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15_U.S.C._§_204-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-15_U.S.C._§_204_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-15_U.S.C._§_204_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>It shall be lawful throughout the United States of America to employ the weights and measures of the metric system; and no contract or dealing, or pleading in any court, shall be deemed invalid or liable to objection because the weights or measures expressed or referred to therein are weights or measures of the metric system.</i> (<a href="/wiki/Title_15_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 15 of the United States Code">15 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/15/204" rel="nofollow">§ 204</a>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Here 'official status' means that the SI is recognized in <i>some</i> way by the laws and regulations of the country. In many countries, this means that using the SI units is mandatory for most commercial and administrative purposes (e.g. <a href="/wiki/European_units_of_measurement_directives" title="European units of measurement directives">in the European Union</a>). On the other hand, when it comes to the US, 'official status' means that federal law <i>specifically</i> allows, but doesn't <i>require,</i> the SI units to be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15_U.S.C._§_204_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-15_U.S.C._§_204-25">[l]</a></sup> In fact, federal law even states that it is <i>the declared policy of the United States to designate the metric system of measurement as the preferred system of weights and measures for United States trade and commerce</i> (<a href="/wiki/Title_15_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 15 of the United States Code">15 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/15/205b" rel="nofollow">§ 205b</a>).<br/>See <a href="/wiki/Metrication" title="Metrication">metrication</a> for more information.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This includes <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_the_United_States" title="Metrication in the United States">the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_Canada" title="Metrication in Canada">Canada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metrication_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Metrication in the United Kingdom">the United Kingdom</a>, despite the fact these three countries also continue to use their <a href="/wiki/Imperial_and_US_customary_measurement_systems" title="Imperial and US customary measurement systems">customary systems</a> to various degrees.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the precise definition of coherence is <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(units_of_measurement)#Dimension-related_coherence" title="Coherence (units of measurement)">complicated</a>, the basic idea is that mathematical relations between the units for quantities should mirror the mathematical relations between the corresponding quantities themselves. For example, the coherent unit of <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> is equal to the volume of a cube whose sides are one unit of length; the coherent unit of <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> is equal to the pressure exerted by a unit-magnitude force over a surface of unit area; etc. As an example of lack of coherence, consider how, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">US customary system</a>, the units of fluid volume are related to the units of length. The principal units of length are inches, feet, yards, and miles; meanwhile, the principal units of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_fluid_ounce" title="US fluid ounce">fluid volume</a> are based on the (US) <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">gallon</a>, which, at 231 cubic inches, is not a cubic inch, or a cubic foot, or a cubic yard, or a cubic mile (note that 231=3×7×11).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the SI unit of <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> is the metre per second, m⋅s<sup>−1</sup>; of <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a> is the metre per second squared, m⋅s<sup>−2</sup>; etc. These can also be written as m/s and m/s<sup>2</sup>, respectively.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22derived-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-22derived_30-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-22derived_30-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">For example the <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">newton</a> (N), the unit of <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>, equivalent to kg⋅m⋅s<sup>−2</sup>; the <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> (J), the unit of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, equivalent to kg⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup>, etc. The most recently named derived unit, the <a href="/wiki/Katal" title="Katal">katal</a>, was defined in 1999.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29combin-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-29combin_31-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-29combin_31-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the recommended unit for the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field strength</a> is the volt per metre, V/m, where the <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volt</a> is the derived unit for <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">electric potential difference</a>. The volt per metre is equal to kg⋅m⋅s<sup>−3</sup>⋅A<sup>−1</sup> when expressed in terms of base units.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This must be one of 29 coherent units with a separate name and symbol, i.e. either one of the seven base units or one of the 22 coherent derived units with special names and symbols.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the coherent SI unit of length is the metre, about the height of kitchen counter (just over <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6999914400000000000♠"></span>3 ft</span>). But for driving distances, one would normally use <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">kilometres</a>, where one kilometre is 1000 metres; here the metric prefix '<a href="/wiki/Kilo-" title="Kilo-">kilo-</a>' (symbol 'k') stands for a factor of 1000. On the other hand, for <a href="/wiki/Tailor" title="Tailor">tailoring</a> measurements, one would usually use <a href="/wiki/Centimetre" title="Centimetre">centimetres</a>, where one centimetre is 1/100 of a metre; here the metric prefix '<a href="/wiki/Centi-" title="Centi-">centi-</a>' (symbol 'c') stands for a factor of 1/100.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Non-coherent, customary systems have another tendency, well-illustrated by the US customary system. In that system, some liquid <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a> are measured neither in the coherent units of volume (e.g. cubic inches) nor in gallons, but in <a href="/wiki/Barrel" title="Barrel">barrels</a>. Furthermore, the size of the barrel depends on the commodity: it means 31 US gallons for <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[14]</a></sup> but 42 gallons for <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[15]</a></sup>
So different units for one and the same quantity (e.g. volume) are used depending on what is being measured, and these different units may not be related to each other in any obvious way—even if they have the same name.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Meaning that different units for a given quantity, such as length, are related by factors of 10. Therefore, calculations involve the simple process of moving the decimal point to the right or to the left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAndMetricSystem_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-USAndMetricSystem-10">[8]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the terms <i>the metric system</i> and <i>the SI</i> are often used as synonyms, there are in fact many mutually incompatible metric systems. Moreover, there exist metric units that are not recognised by any larger metric system. See <i><a href="#Metric_units_that_are_not_recognised_by_the_SI">§ Metric units that are not recognised by the SI</a></i>, below.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As of May 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, only for the following countries is it <a href="/wiki/Metrication#Overview" title="Metrication">uncertain whether the SI has any official status</a>: <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Federated_States_of_Micronesia" title="Federated States of Micronesia">Federated States of Micronesia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">Marshall Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the US, the history of legislation begins with the <a href="/wiki/Metric_Act_of_1866" title="Metric Act of 1866">Metric Act of 1866</a>, which legally protected use of the metric system in commerce. The first section is still part of US law (<a href="/wiki/Title_15_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 15 of the United States Code">15 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/15/204" rel="nofollow">§ 204</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15_U.S.C._§_204_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-15_U.S.C._§_204-25">[l]</a></sup> In 1875, the US became one of the original signatories of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>. In 1893, the <a href="/wiki/Mendenhall_Order" title="Mendenhall Order">Mendenhall Order</a> stated that <i>the Office of Weights and Measures ... will in the future regard the International Prototype Metre and Kilogramme as fundamental standards, and the customary units — the yard and the pound — will be derived therefrom in accordance with the Act of July 28, 1866.</i> In 1954, the US adopted the <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">International Nautical Mile</a>, which is defined as exactly <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003185200000000000♠"></span>1852 m</span>, in lieu of the US Nautical Mile, defined as <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003185324496000000♠"></span>6<span style="margin-left:.25em;">080</span>.20 ft</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7003185324800000000♠"></span>1<span style="margin-left:.25em;">853</span>.248 m</span>. In 1959, the US National Bureau of Standards officially adapted the <a href="/wiki/International_yard_and_pound" title="International yard and pound">International yard and pound</a>, which are defined exactly in terms of the metre and the kilogram. In 1968, the Metric Study Act (Pub. L. 90-472, August 9, 1968, 82 Stat. 693) authorised a three-year study of systems of measurement in the US, with particular emphasis on the feasibility of adopting the SI. The <a href="/wiki/Metric_Conversion_Act" title="Metric Conversion Act">Metric Conversion Act</a> of 1975 followed, later amended by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnibus_Trade_and_Competitiveness_Act_of_1988" title="Omnibus Trade and Competitiveness Act of 1988">Omnibus Trade and Competitiveness Act of 1988</a>, the Savings in Construction Act of 1996, and the Department of Energy High-End Computing Revitalization Act of 2004. As a result of all these acts, the US current law (<a href="/wiki/Title_15_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 15 of the United States Code">15 U.S.C.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/15/205b" rel="nofollow">§ 205b</a>) states that <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is therefore the declared policy of the United States-
</p><p>(1) to designate the metric system of measurement as the preferred system of weights and measures for United States trade and commerce;
</p><p>(2) to require that each Federal agency, by a date certain and to the extent economically feasible by the end of the fiscal year 1992, use the metric system of measurement in its procurements, grants, and other business-related activities, except to the extent that such use is impractical or is likely to cause significant inefficiencies or loss of markets to United States firms, such as when foreign competitors are producing competing products in non-metric units;
</p><p>(3) to seek out ways to increase understanding of the metric system of measurement through educational information and guidance and in Government publications; and
</p><p>
(4) to permit the continued use of traditional systems of weights and measures in non-business activities.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There are differences between the US customary system and the United Kingdom's imperial system. For example, the imperial <a href="/wiki/Gallon" title="Gallon">gallon</a> is about 20% larger than the US gallon.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">And have been defined in terms of the SI's metric predecessors since <a href="/wiki/International_yard_and_pound#History" title="International yard and pound">at least the 1890s</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See e.g. <a href="/wiki/Catty" title="Catty">here</a> for the various definitions of the catty, a traditional Chinese unit of mass, in various places across East and Southeast Asia. Similarly, see <a href="/wiki/Japanese_units_of_measurement" title="Japanese units of measurement">this article on the traditional Japanese units of measurement</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Indian_units_of_measurement" title="Indian units of measurement">this one on the traditional Indian units of measurement</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-French_CIPM-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-French_CIPM_44-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-French_CIPM_44-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Comité international des poids et mesures</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-SI_Brochure_short-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-SI_Brochure_short_45-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-SI_Brochure_short_45-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>SI Brochure</i> for short. As of May 2020<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_System_of_Units&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the latest edition is the ninth, published in 2019. It is Ref.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup> of this article.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BIPM_from_French-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-BIPM_from_French_46-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BIPM_from_French_46-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">from <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Bureau international des poids et mesures</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The latter are formalised in the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Quantities" title="International System of Quantities">International System of Quantities</a> (ISQ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title='Page / location: 129
Quotation : "The ISO/IEC 80000 series of Standards specify base and derived quantities which necessarily correspond to the SI base and derived units."'>129</span> </sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The choice of which and even how many quantities to use as base quantities is not fundamental or even unique – it is a matter of convention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title='Page / location: 126
Quotation : "... the choice of the base units was never unique, but grew historically and became familiar to users of the SI"'>126</span> </sup>  For example, four base quantities could have been chosen as velocity, angular momentum, electric charge and energy.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Here are some examples of coherent derived SI units: the unit of <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">metre per second</a>, with the symbol <span class="nowrap">m/s</span>; the unit of <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared" title="Metre per second squared">metre per second squared</a>, with the symbol <span class="nowrap">m/s<sup>2</sup></span>; etc.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A useful property of a coherent system is that when the numerical values of physical quantities are expressed in terms of the units of the system, then the equations between the numerical values have exactly the same form, including numerical factors, as the corresponding equations between the physical quantities;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISO80000-1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISO80000-1-50">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6">: 6 </span></sup> An example may be useful to clarify this. Suppose we are given an equation relating some <a href="/wiki/Physical_quantity" title="Physical quantity">physical quantities</a>, e.g. <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>{<i>m</i>}{<i>v</i>}<sup>2</sup></span>, expressing the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i></span> in terms of the mass <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i></span> and the velocity <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span>. Choose a system of units, and let <span class="texhtml">{<i>T</i>}</span>, <span class="texhtml">{<i>m</i>}</span>, and <span class="texhtml">{<i>v</i>}</span> be the numerical values of <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span> when expressed in that system of units. If the system is coherent, then the numerical values will obey the same equation (including numerical factors) as the physical quantities, i.e. we will have that <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>{<i>m</i>}{<i>v</i>}<sup>2</sup></span>. Therefore, SI units can be converted without numerical factors: <span class="texhtml">1 J = 1 N·m = 1 C·V = 1 W·s</span>.<br/>On the other hand, if the chosen system of units is not coherent, this property may fail. For example, the following is not a coherent system: one where energy is measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calories" title="Calories">calories</a>, while mass and velocity are measured in their SI units. After all, in that case, <span class="texhtml"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>{<i>m</i>}{<i>v</i>}<sup>2</sup></span> will give a numerical value whose meaning is the kinetic energy when expressed in joules, and that numerical value is different, by a factor of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000418400000000000♠"></span>4.184</span>, from the numerical value when the kinetic energy is expressed in calories. Thus, in that system, the equation satisfied by the numerical values is instead <span class="texhtml">{<i>T</i>} = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4.184</span></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span>{<i>m</i>}{<i>v</i>}<sup>2</sup></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Which define the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Quantities" title="International System of Quantities">International System of Quantities</a> (ISQ).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is correct to say that an SI base unit (like the metre) is a coherent unit for its corresponding physical quantity. Recall that the <i>set of coherent SI units</i> consists of the base units and the coherent derived units. This usage is consistent with the definition of a coherent unit as one that is equal to 'a product of powers of the base units with a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/prefactor" title="wiktionary:prefactor">prefactor</a> of 1'. After all, each base unit is obviously so representable—it is equal to itself to the power of 1 and with a prefactor of 1.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One kilometre is about 0.62 <a href="/wiki/Mile" title="Mile">miles</a>, a length equal to about two and a half laps around a typical athletic track. Walking at a moderate pace for one hour, an adult human will cover about five kilometres (about three miles). The distance from London, UK, to Paris, France is about <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005350000000000000♠"></span>350 km</span>; from London to New York, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7006560000000000000♠"></span>5600 km</span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In other words, given any base unit or any coherent derived unit with a special name and symbol.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-prefixkg-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-prefixkg_56-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-prefixkg_56-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">For historical reasons, names and symbols for decimal multiples and sub-multiples of the unit of mass are formed as if it is the <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a> which is the base unit, i.e. by attaching prefix names and symbols, respectively, to the unit name "gram" and the unit symbol "g". For example, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6994100000000000000♠"></span>10<sup>−6</sup> kg</span> is written as milligram, <span class="nowrap">mg</span>, not as microkilogram, <span class="nowrap">μkg</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 144">: 144 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This last statement in fact applies to <i>all</i> SI units, not only those with special names and symbols. Consider the example of the SI units of <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torque</a>. Because the SI does not have a unit with a special name and symbol for torque, its coherent SI unit is the <a href="/wiki/Newton-metre" title="Newton-metre">newton-metre</a>, N⋅m. The following are some examples of <i>non-coherent</i> SI units of torque: N⋅mm, kN⋅μm, mN⋅cm, etc. Note that these non-coherent units are obtained from the original coherent unit by replacing some (or all) of the units with special names and symbols that are present in the original coherent unit by their decimal multiples or submultiples. But then these different powers of ten combine into one overall power of ten. For example, kN⋅μm = (10<sup>3</sup> N)⋅(10<sup>−6</sup>  m) = 10<sup>3–6</sup> N⋅m = 10<sup>−3</sup> N⋅m.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note, however, that there is a special group of units that are called <i>non-SI units accepted for use with SI,</i> most of which are not decimal multiples of the corresponding SI units; see <a href="#Non-SI_units_accepted_for_use_with_SI">below</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the <i>SI Brochure</i> states,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-18"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 140">: 140 </span></sup> <i>this applies not only to technical texts, but also, for example, to measuring instruments (i.e. the instrument read-out needs to indicate both the unit and the quantity measured).</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Customarily, however, <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge#National_coverage_and_modern_gauges" title="Rain gauge">rainfall is measured</a> in non-coherent SI units such as millimetres in height collected on each square metre during a certain period, equivalent to litres per square metre.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Even base units; the mole was added as a base SI unit only in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-21"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 156">: 156 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See the next section for why this type of definition is considered advantageous.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="nowrap">Their exactly defined values are as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-24"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 128">: 128 </span></sup></span><br/>
