<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A <i>mise en pratique</i> is <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> for 'putting into practice; implementation'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_mise_en_pratique_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIST_mise_en_pratique-67">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reverso_Context_mise_en_pratique_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reverso_Context_mise_en_pratique-68">[21]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Second_as_exception-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Second_as_exception_71-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Second_as_exception_71-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The sole exception is the definition of the second, which is still given not in terms of fixed values of fundamental constants but in terms of a particular property of a particular naturally occurring object, the caesium atom. And indeed, it has been clear for some time that relatively soon, by using <a href="/wiki/Atomic_clock#Research" title="Atomic clock">atoms other than caesium</a>, it will be possible to have definitions of the second that are more precise than the current one. Taking advantage of these more precise methods will necessitate the change in the definition of the second, probably sometime around the year 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Changing_def_of_the_second_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Changing_def_of_the_second-77">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 196">: 196 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGPM2022-Draft_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-CGPM2022-Draft-78">[24]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant_72-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant_72-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Again, except for the second, as explained in the previous note.<br/>
The second may eventually get fixed by defining an exact value for yet another fundamental constant (whose derived unit includes the second), for example the <a href="/wiki/Rydberg_constant" title="Rydberg constant">Rydberg constant</a>. For this to happen, the <a href="/wiki/Measurement_uncertainty" title="Measurement uncertainty">uncertainty in the measurement</a> of that constant must become so small as to be dominated by the uncertainty in the measurement of whatever <a href="/wiki/Atomic_clock" title="Atomic clock">clock transition</a> frequency is being used to define the second at that point. Once that happens, the definitions will be reversed: the value of the constant will be fixed by definition to an exact value, namely its most recent best measured value, while the clock transition frequency will become a quantity whose value is no longer fixed by definition but which has to be measured. Unfortunately, it is unlikely that this will happen in the foreseeable future, because presently there are no promising strategies for measuring any additional fundamental constants with the necessary precision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Changing_def_of_the_second_Rydberg_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Changing_def_of_the_second_Rydberg-79">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 4112–3">: 4112–3 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The one exception being the definition of the second; see Notes <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Second_as_exception_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Second_as_exception-71">[aw]</a></sup> and <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_second_in_terms_of_a_constant-72">[ax]</a></sup> in the following section.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">To see this, recall that <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap">Hz</span> = <span class="nowrap">s<sup>−1</sup></span></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap">J</span> = <span class="nowrap">kg</span>⋅<span class="nowrap">m<sup>2</sup></span>⋅<span class="nowrap">s<sup>−2</sup></span>.</span> Thus,<br/> <span class="nowrap">(<span class="nowrap">Hz</span>) (<span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>) / (<span class="nowrap">m/s</span>)<sup>2</sup><br/> = (<span class="nowrap">s<sup>−1</sup></span>) [(<span class="nowrap">kg</span>⋅<span class="nowrap">m<sup>2</sup></span>⋅<span class="nowrap">s<sup>−2</sup></span>)⋅<span class="nowrap">s</span>] (<span class="nowrap">m</span>⋅<span class="nowrap">s<sup>−1</sup></span>)<sup>−2</sup><br/> = <span class="nowrap">s</span><sup>(−1−2+1+2)</sup>⋅<span class="nowrap">m</span><sup>(2–2)</sup>⋅<span class="nowrap">kg</span> <br/> = <span class="nowrap">kg</span>,</span><br/> since all the powers of metres and seconds cancel out. It can further be shown that <span class="nowrap">(<span class="nowrap">Hz</span>) (<span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>) / (<span class="nowrap">m/s</span>)<sup>2</sup></span> is the <i>only</i> combination of powers of the units of the defining constants (that is, the only combination of powers of <span class="nowrap">Hz</span>, <span class="nowrap">m/s</span>, <span class="nowrap">J⋅s</span>, <span class="nowrap">C</span>, <span class="nowrap">J/K</span>, <span class="nowrap">mol<sup>−1</sup></span>, and <span class="nowrap">lm/W</span>) that results in the kilogram.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Namely,<br/> <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 Hz</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml">Δ<i>ν</i><sub>Cs</sub></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7009919263177000000♠"></span>9<span style="margin-left:.25em;">192</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">631</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">770</span></span></span></span><br/> <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 m/s</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span></span></span></span> , and<br/> <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 J⋅s</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6966662607015000000♠"></span>6.626<span style="margin-left:.25em;">070</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">15</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−34</sup></span></span></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The SI Brochure prefers to write the relationship between the kilogram and the defining constants directly, without going through the intermediary step of defining <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 Hz</span>, <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 m/s</span>, and <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 J⋅s</span>, like this:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-27"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 131">: 131 </span></sup> <span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 kg</span> = <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">(<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008299792458000000♠"></span>299<span style="margin-left:.25em;">792</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">458</span></span>)<sup>2</sup></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">(<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6966662607015000000♠"></span>6.626<span style="margin-left:.25em;">070</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">15</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−34</sup></span>)(<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7009919263177000000♠"></span>9<span style="margin-left:.25em;">192</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">631</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">770</span></span>)</span></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num"><span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span><span class="texhtml">Δ<i>ν</i><sub>Cs</sub></span></span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den"><span class="texhtml"><i>c</i></span><sup>2</sup></span></span>.</span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, from 1889 until 1960, the metre was defined as the length of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Prototype_Metre" title="International Prototype Metre">International Prototype Metre</a>, a particular bar made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platinum-iridium_alloy" title="Platinum-iridium alloy">platinum-iridium alloy</a> that was (and still is) kept at the <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a>, located in the <a href="/wiki/Pavillon_de_Breteuil" title="Pavillon de Breteuil">Pavillon de Breteuil</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint-Cloud" title="Saint-Cloud">Saint-Cloud</a>, France, near Paris. The final artefact-based definition of the metre, which stood from 1927 to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metre#Krypton_standard" title="History of the metre">redefinition of the metre in 1960</a>, read as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIBrochure9thEd_1-30"><a href="#cite_note-SIBrochure9thEd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 159">: 159 </span></sup> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The unit of length is the metre, defined by the distance, at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="5000000000000000000♠"></span>0°</span>, between the axes of the two central lines marked on the bar of platinum-iridium kept at the Bureau International des Poids et Mesures and declared Prototype of the metre by the 1st Conférence Générale des Poids et Mesures, this bar being subject to standard atmospheric pressure and supported on two cylinders of at least one centimetre diameter, symmetrically placed in the same horizontal plane at a distance of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6999571000000000000♠"></span>571 mm</span> from each other.</p></blockquote> The '<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="5000000000000000000♠"></span>0°</span>' refers to the temperature of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002273149999999999♠"></span>0 °C</span>. The support requirements represent the <a href="/wiki/Airy_points" title="Airy points">Airy points</a> of the prototype—the points, separated by <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">7</span></span> of the total length of the bar, at which the <a href="/wiki/Bending" title="Bending">bending</a> or <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/droop" title="wikt:droop">droop</a> of the bar is minimised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[27]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The latter was called the 'quadrant', the length of a meridian from the equator to the North Pole. The originally chosen meridian was the <a href="/wiki/Paris_meridian" title="Paris meridian">Paris meridian</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the time 'weight' and 'mass' were not always <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight" title="Mass versus weight">carefully distinguished</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> This volume is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6994100000000000000♠"></span>1 cm<sup>3</sup></span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6994100000000000000♠"></span>1 mL</span>, which is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6994100000000000000♠"></span>1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−6</sup> m<sup>3</sup></span>. Thus, the original definition of mass used not the coherent unit of volume (which would be the <span class="nowrap">m<sup>3</sup></span>) but a decimal submultiple of it.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Indeed, the original idea of the metric system was to define all units using only natural and universally available measurable quantities. For example, the original definition of the unit of length, the metre, was a definite fraction (one ten-millionth) of the length of a quarter of the Earth's meridian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[bd]</a></sup> Once the metre was defined, one could define the unit of volume as the volume of a cube whose sides are one unit of length. And once the unit of volume was determined, the unit of mass could be defined as the mass of a unit of volume of some convenient substance at standard conditions. In fact, the original definition of the gram was 'the absolute weight<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[be]</a></sup> of a volume of pure water equal to the cube of the hundredth part of a metre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[bf]</a></sup> and at the temperature of melting ice.' <br/><br/>However, it soon became apparent that these particular 'natural' realisations of the units of length and mass simply could not, at that time, be as precise (and as convenient to access) as the needs of science, technology, and commerce demanded. Therefore, prototypes were adopted instead. Care was taken to manufacture the prototypes so that they would be as close as possible, given the available science and technology of the day, to the idealised 'natural' realisations. But once the prototypes were completed, the units of length and mass became equal by definition to these prototypes (see <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A8tre_des_Archives" title="Mètre des Archives">Mètre des Archives</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilogramme_des_Archives" title="Kilogramme des Archives">Kilogramme des Archives</a></i>). <br/><br/> Nevertheless, throughout the history of the SI, one keeps seeing expressions of hope that one day, one would be able to dispense with the prototypes and define all units in terms of standards found in nature. The first such standard was the second. It was never defined using a prototype, being originally defined as 1/<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004864000000000000♠"></span>86<span style="margin-left:.25em;">400</span></span> of the length of a day (since there are 60 s/min × 60 min/hr × 24 hr/day = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7004864000000000000♠"></span>86<span style="margin-left:.25em;">400</span></span> s/day).  As we mentioned, the vision of defining all units in terms of universally available natural standards was at last fulfilled in 2019, when the sole remaining prototype used by the SI, the one for the kilogram, was finally retired.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The following references are useful for identifying the authors of the preceding reference: Ref.,,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Memoir_of_Francis_Baily_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Memoir_of_Francis_Baily-88">[29]</a></sup> Ref.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royal_Commission_Munutes_1874_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Royal_Commission_Munutes_1874-89">[30]</a></sup> and Ref.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edinburgh_Review_Report_on_Restoration_of_Standards_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edinburgh_Review_Report_on_Restoration_of_Standards-90">[31]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834_92-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834_92-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">As happened with British standards for length and mass in 1834, when they were lost or damaged beyond the point of useability in a great fire known as the <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_Parliament" title="Burning of Parliament">burning of Parliament</a>. A commission of eminent scientists was assembled to recommend the steps to be taken for the restoration of the standards, and in its report, it described the destruction caused by the fire as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Report_on_Restoration_of_Standards_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Report_on_Restoration_of_Standards-87">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[bh]</a></sup>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We shall in the first place describe the state of the Standards recovered from the ruins of the House of Commons, as ascertained in our inspection of them made on 1st June, 1838, at the Journal Office, where they are preserved under the care of Mr. James Gudge, Principal Clerk of the Journal Office. The following list, taken by ourselves from inspection, was compared with a list produced by Mr. Gudge, and stated by him to have been made by Mr. Charles Rowland, one of the Clerks of the Journal Office, immediately after the fire, and was found to agree with it. Mr. Gudge stated that no other Standards of Length or Weight were in his custody.
</p><p>No. 1. A brass bar marked "Standard [G. II. crown emblem] Yard, 1758", which on examination was found to have its right hand stud perfect, with the point and line visible, but with its left hand stud completely melted out, a hole only remaining. The bar was somewhat bent, and discoloured in every part.
</p><p>No. 2. A brass bar with a projecting cock at each end, forming a bed for the trial of yard-measures; discoloured.
</p><p>No. 3. A brass bar marked "Standard [G. II. crown emblem] Yard, 1760", from which the left hand stud was completely melted out, and which in other respects was in the same condition as No. 1.
</p><p>No. 4. A yard-bed similar to No. 2; discoloured.
</p><p>No. 5. A weight of the form [drawing of a weight] marked [2 lb. T. 1758], apparently of brass or copper; much discoloured.
</p><p>No. 6. A weight marked in the same manner for 4 lbs., in the same state.
</p><p>No. 7. A weight similar to No. 6, with a hollow space at its base, which appeared at first sight to have been originally filled with some soft metal that had been now melted out, but which on a rough trial was found to have nearly the same weight as No. 6.
</p><p>No. 8. A similar weight of 8 lbs., similarly marked (with the alteration of 8 lbs. for 4 lbs.), and in the same state.
</p><p>No. 9. Another exactly like No. 8.
</p><p>Nos. 10 and 11. Two weights of 16 lbs., similarly marked.
</p><p>Nos. 12 and 13. Two weights of 32 lbs., similarly marked.
</p><p>No. 14. A weight with a triangular ring-handle, marked "S.F. 1759 17 lbs. 8 dwts. Troy", apparently intended to represent the stone of 14 lbs. avoirdupois, allowing 7008 troy grains to each avoirdupois pound.
</p><p>
It appears from this list that the bar adopted in the <a href="/wiki/Weights_and_Measures_Acts_(UK)#19th_century" title="Weights and Measures Acts (UK)">Act 5th Geo. IV., cap. 74</a>, sect. 1, for the legal standard of one yard, (No. 3 of the preceding list), is so far injured, that it is impossible to ascertain from it, with the most moderate accuracy, the statutable length of one yard. The legal standard of one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troy_pound" title="Troy pound">troy pound</a> is missing. We have therefore to report that it is absolutely necessary that steps be taken for the formation and legalising of new Standards of Length and Weight.</p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Indeed, one of the motivations for the 2019 redefinition of the SI was the <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_of_the_Kilogram#Stability_of_the_IPK" title="International Prototype of the Kilogram">instability of the artefact</a> that served as the definition of the kilogram. 
<br/><br/>
Before that, one of the reasons the United States started <a href="/wiki/Mendenhall_Order" title="Mendenhall Order">defining the yard in terms of the metre</a> in 1893 was that<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_Address_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer_Address-93">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 381">: 381 </span></sup> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[t]he bronze yard No. 11, which was an exact copy of the British imperial yard both in form and material, had shown changes when compared with the imperial yard in 1876 and 1888 which could not reasonably be said to be entirely due to changes in No. 11. Suspicion as to the constancy of the length of the British standard was therefore aroused.</p></blockquote> In the above, the bronze yard No. 11 is one of two copies of the new British standard yard that were sent to the US in 1856, after Britain completed the manufacture of new imperial standards to replace those lost in the fire of 1834 (see <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burning_of_imperial_standards_1834-92">[bi]</a></sup>). As standards of length, the new yards, especially bronze No. 11, were far superior to the standard the US had been using up to that point, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Troughton_scale" title="Troughton scale">Troughton scale</a>. They were therefore accepted by the Office of Weights and Measures (a predecessor of <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">NIST</a>) as the standards of the United States. They were twice taken to England and recompared with the imperial yard, in 1876 and in 1888, and, as mentioned above, measurable discrepancies were found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_Address_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer_Address-93">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 381">: 381 </span></sup><br/>
<p>In 1890, as a signatory of the <a href="/wiki/Metre_Convention" title="Metre Convention">Metre Convention</a>, the US received two copies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Prototype_Metre" title="International Prototype Metre">International Prototype Metre</a>, the construction of which represented the most advanced ideas of standards of the time. Therefore it seemed that US measures would have greater stability and higher accuracy by accepting the international metre as fundamental standard, which was formalised in 1893 by the <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Mendenhall_Order" title="Mendenhall Order">Mendenhall Order</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_Address_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer_Address-93">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 379–81">: 379–81 </span></sup></span>
</p>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As mentioned above, it is all but certain that the defining constant <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta \nu _{\text{Cs}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

However, it is not excluded that some of the other defining constants would eventually have to be replaced as well. For example, the elementary charge <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span> corresponds to a coupling strength of the electromagnetic force via the <a href="/wiki/Fine-structure_constant" title="Fine-structure constant">fine-structure constant</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Some theories predict that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

The presently known experimental limits of the maximum possible variation of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Nevertheless, if the fine-structure constant turns out to slightly vary over time, science and technology may in the future advance to a point where such changes become measurable. At that point, one might consider replacing, for the purposes of defining the SI, the elementary charge with some other quantity, the choice of which will be informed by what we learn about the time variation of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \alpha }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is evident from the formula <span class="texhtml"><i>P</i> = <i>I</i> <i>V</i></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The unit is named after <a href="/wiki/Anders_Celsius" title="Anders Celsius">Anders Celsius</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-generalrules-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-generalrules_150-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-generalrules_150-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Except where specifically noted, these rules are common to both the SI Brochure and the NIST brochure.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the United States' <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a> (NIST) has produced a version of the CGPM document (NIST SP 330) which clarifies usage for English-language publications that use <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">American English</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Translation-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Translation_158-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This term is a <a href="#Metre_Convention">translation</a> of the official [French] text of the SI Brochure.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-185"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-185">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The strength of the Earth's magnetic field was designated 1 G (gauss) at the surface (<span class="nowrap">= 1 cm<sup>−1/2</sup>⋅g<sup>1/2</sup>⋅s<sup>−1</sup></span>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-187"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-187">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Argentina, Austria-Hungary, Belgium, Brazil, Denmark, France, German Empire, Italy, Peru, Portugal, Russia, Spain, Sweden and Norway, Switzerland, Ottoman Empire, United States, and Venezuela.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-188">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The text "<i>Des comparaisons périodiques des étalons nationaux avec les prototypes internationaux</i>" (English: <span lang="en">the periodic comparisons of national standards with the international prototypes</span>) in article 6.3 of the <a class="external text" href="http://www.bipm.org/utils/common/documents/official/metre-convention.pdf" rel="nofollow">Metre Convention</a> distinguishes between the words "standard" (<a class="external text" href="http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/188962?rskey=CZQ845&amp;result=1#eid" rel="nofollow">OED: "The legal magnitude of a unit of measure or weight"</a>) and "prototype" (<a class="external text" href="http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/153327?rskey=G9OW8z&amp;result=1#eid" rel="nofollow">OED: "an original on which something is modelled"</a>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-191"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-191">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
These included:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">General Conference on Weights and Measures</a> (<i>Conférence générale des poids et mesures</i> or CGPM)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Committee_for_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Committee for Weights and Measures">International Committee for Weights and Measures</a> (<i>Comité international des poids et mesures</i> or CIPM)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">International Bureau of Weights and Measures</a> (<i>Bureau international des poids et mesures</i> or BIPM) – an international metrology centre at <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A8vres" title="Sèvres">Sèvres</a> in France that has custody of the International prototype kilogram, provides <a href="/wiki/Metrology" title="Metrology">metrology</a> services for the CGPM and CIPM.</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Pferd</i> is <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> for "horse" and <i>Stärke</i> is German for "strength" or "power". The Pferdestärke is the power needed to raise 75 kg against gravity at the rate of one metre per second. (<span class="nowrap">1 PS = 0.985 HP</span>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is known as the International Prototype of the Kilogram.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-206"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-206">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Meaning, they are neither part of the SI nor one of the <a href="/wiki/Non-SI_units_mentioned_in_the_SI#Units_officially_accepted_for_use_with_the_SI" title="Non-SI units mentioned in the SI">non-SI units accepted for use</a> with that system.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Almost invariably either the meter or the centimeter.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-208">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All major systems of units in which force rather than mass is a base unit are of a type known as <i>gravitational</i> system (also known as <i>technical</i> or <i>engineering</i> system). In the most prominent <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_metric_system" title="Gravitational metric system">metric example</a> of such a system, the unit of force is taken to be the <a href="/wiki/Kilogram-force" title="Kilogram-force">kilogram-force</a> (<span class="nowrap">kp</span>), which is the <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight" title="Mass versus weight">weight</a> of the <a href="/wiki/International_Prototype_of_the_Kilogram" title="International Prototype of the Kilogram">standard kilogram</a> under <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">standard gravity</a>, <span class="texhtml"><i>g</i></span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000980665000000000♠"></span>9.806<span style="margin-left:.25em;">65</span> m/s<sup>2</sup></span>. The unit of mass is then a derived unit, defined as the mass that is accelerated at a rate of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 m/s<sup>2</sup></span> when acted upon by a net force of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000980665000000000♠"></span>1 kp</span>; often called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyl_(mass)" title="Hyl (mass)">hyl</a>, it therefore has a value of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 hyl</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000980665000000000♠"></span>9.806<span style="margin-left:.25em;">65</span> kg</span>, so that it is not a decimal multiple of the gram.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-209">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Having said that, some units are recognised by all metric systems. The second is a base unit in all of them. The metre is recognised in all of them, either as the base unit of length or as a decimal multiple or submultiple of the base unit of length. On the other hand, not every metric system recognises the gram as a unit (either the base unit or a decimal multiple of the base unit). In particular, in <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_metric_system" title="Gravitational metric system">gravitational metric systems</a>, the unit of force (gram-force or kilogram-force) replaces the unit of mass as a base unit. The unit of mass is then a derived unit, defined as the mass that, when acted upon by a net unit force, is accelerated at the unit rate (i.e. at a rate of 1 base unit of length<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[cl]</a></sup> per second squared).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[cm]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Corresponding_not_equal-213"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Corresponding_not_equal_213-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Corresponding_not_equal_213-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Corresponding_not_equal_213-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Interconversion between different systems of units is usually straightforward; however, the units for electricity and magnetism are an exception, and a surprising amount of care is required. The problem is that, in general, the physical quantities that go by the same name and play the same role in the CGS-ESU, CGS-EMU, and SI—e.g. 'electric charge', 'electric field strength', etc.—do not merely have different units in the three systems; technically speaking, they are actually different physical quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page_1970_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Page_1970-210">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title='Page / location: 422
Quotation : "Since the corresponding equations in different systems are not identical, the symbols in them must represent (slightly) different quantities, i.e. different mathematical models of invariant physical phenomena."'>422</span> </sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page_1970_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-Page_1970-210">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title='Page / location: 423
Quotation : "The precise statement involved in converting from emu to SI (the modern International System) is as follows. A magnetic field described by 𝘏 = 1 Oe is also described by 𝘏 = 1000/4π A/m. These statements describe the 𝘴𝘢𝘮𝘦 𝘱𝘩𝘦𝘯𝘰𝘮𝘦𝘯𝘰𝘯 in terms of different quantities."'>423</span> </sup> Consider 'electric charge', which in each of the three systems can be identified as the quantity two instances of which enter in the numerator of <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">Coulomb's law</a> (as that law is written in each system). This identification produces three different physical quantities: the 'CGS-ESU charge', the 'CGS-EMU charge', and the 'SI charge'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISO80000-6_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISO80000-6-211">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 35">: 35 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page_1970_210-2"><a href="#cite_note-Page_1970-210">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 423">: 423 </span></sup> They even have different dimensions when expressed in terms of the base dimensions: mass<sup>1/2</sup> × length<sup>3/2</sup> × time<sup>−1</sup> for the CGS-ESU charge, mass<sup>1/2</sup> × length<sup>1/2</sup> for the CGS-EMU charge, and current × time for the SI  charge (where, in the SI, the dimension of current is independent of those of mass, length, and time). On the other hand, these three quantities are clearly quantifying the same underlying physical phenomenon. Thus, we say not that 'one abcoulomb <i>equals</i> ten coulomb', but rather that 'one abcoulomb <i>corresponds to</i> ten coulomb',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Page_1970_210-3"><a href="#cite_note-Page_1970-210">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">: <span class="tooltip tooltip-dashed" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed;" title="Page / location: 423
Quotation : &quot;Similarly, it is incorrect to state 'one abampere 𝘦𝘲𝘶𝘢𝘭𝘴 ten amperes', 'one tesla 𝘪𝘴 ten kilogauss', etc.; the correct verb is 𝘤𝘰𝘳𝘳𝘦𝘴𝘱𝘰𝘯𝘥𝘴 𝘵𝘰.&quot;">423</span> </sup> written as <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 abC</span> ≘ <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001100000000000000♠"></span>10 C</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISO80000-6_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISO80000-6-211">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 35">: 35 </span></sup> By that we mean, 'if the CGS-EMU electric charge is measured to have the magnitude of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>1 abC</span>, then the SI electric charge will have the magnitude of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001100000000000000♠"></span>10 C</span>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISO80000-6_211-2"><a href="#cite_note-ISO80000-6-211">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 35">: 35 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carron_Babel_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carron_Babel-212">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 57–58">: 57–58 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Neither EMU nor ESU units had sizes that were convenient for practical work of electrical engineers. It was therefore decided to establish a 'practical' system of units, where each unit is an appropriate decimal multiple or submultiple of the corresponding EMU unit, so that the resulting units have convenient sizes <i>and</i> form a coherent system. These practical units were given names derived from the names of eminent scientists, and many of these units—both the names and the magnitudes—were later incorporated into the SI: the volt, the ampere, the ohm, etc.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-stat_and_abstat-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-stat_and_abstat_217-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For several decades, the ESU and EMU units did not have special names; one would just say, for example, <i>the ESU unit of resistance.</i> In 1903, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_E._Kennelly" title="Arthur E. Kennelly">A. E. Kennelly</a> suggested that the names of the EMU units be obtained by prefixing the name of the corresponding 'practical unit' by 'ab-' (short for 'absolute', giving the 'abohm', '<a href="/wiki/Abvolt" title="Abvolt">abvolt</a>', the '<a href="/wiki/Abampere" title="Abampere">abampere</a>', etc.), and that the names of the ESU units be analogously obtained by using the prefix 'abstat-', which was later shortened to 'stat-' (giving the 'statohm', '<a href="/wiki/Statvolt" title="Statvolt">statvolt</a>', '<a href="/wiki/Statampere" title="Statampere">statampere</a>', etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennelly_1903_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennelly_1903-214">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 534–5">: 534–5 </span></sup> In a sense, this brings us back full circle: historically, the magnitudes of the 'practical units' were first defined in terms of the magnitudes of the corresponding units of the EMU system;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[cp]</a></sup> then, Kennelly proposed that the <i>names</i> of units in the ESU and EMU systems be derived from the corresponding names of practical units. Kennelly's naming system was widely used in the US, but, apparently, not in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silsbee_1962_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silsbee_1962-216">[111]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Gaussian_as_blend-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Gaussian_as_blend_218-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Gaussian_as_blend_218-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_units" title="Gaussian units">CGS-Gaussian units</a> are a blend of the CGS-ESU and CGS-EMU, taking units related to magnetism from the latter and all the rest from the former. In addition, the system introduces the gauss as a special name for the CGS-EMU unit maxwell per square centimetre.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Authors often abuse notation slightly and write these with an 'equals' sign ('=') rather than a 'corresponds to' sign ('≘').</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Kennelly_1903-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Kennelly_1903_214-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFKennelly1903"><a href="/wiki/Arthur_E._Kennelly" title="Arthur E. Kennelly">Kennelly, A. E.</a> (July 1903). <a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/books/edition/Transactions/lTFDAQAAMAAJ?gbpv=1&amp;pg=PA1412&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;bsq=%22The%20expedient%20suggests%20itself%20of%20attaching%20the%20prefix%20ab%20or%20abs%20to%20a%20practical%20or%20Q.%20E.%20S.%20unit%2C%20in%20order%20to%20express%20the%20absolute%20or%20corresponding%20C.%20G.%20S.%20magnetic%20unit.%22" rel="nofollow">"Magnetic Units and Other Subjects that Might Occupy Attention at the Next International Electrical Congress"</a>. <i>Transactions of the American Institute of Electrical Engineers</i>. <b>XXII</b>: 529–536. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1109%2FT-AIEE.1903.4764390" rel="nofollow">10.1109/T-AIEE.1903.4764390</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:51634810" rel="nofollow">51634810</a>. <q>[p. 534] The expedient suggests itself of attaching the prefix <i>ab</i> or <i>abs</i> to a practical or Q. E. S. unit, in order to express the absolute or corresponding C. G. S. magnetic unit. … [p. 535] In a comprehensive system of electromagnetic terminology, the electric C. G. S. units should also be christened. They are sometimes referred to in electrical papers, but always in an apologetic, symbolical fashion, owing to the absence of names to cover their nakedness. They might be denoted by the prefix <i>abstat</i>.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Transactions+of+the+American+Institute+of+Electrical+Engineers&amp;rft.atitle=Magnetic+Units+and+Other+Subjects+that+Might+Occupy+Attention+at+the+Next+International+Electrical+Congress&amp;rft.volume=XXII&amp;rft.pages=529-536&amp;rft.date=1903-07&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1109%2FT-AIEE.1903.4764390&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A51634810%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Kennelly&amp;rft.aufirst=A.+E.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.google.com%2Fbooks%2Fedition%2FTransactions%2FlTFDAQAAMAAJ%3Fgbpv%3D1%26pg%3DPA1412%26printsec%3Dfrontcover%26bsq%3D%2522The%2520expedient%2520suggests%2520itself%2520of%2520attaching%2520the%2520prefix%2520ab%2520or%2520abs%2520to%2520a%2520practical%2520or%2520Q.%2520E.%2520S.%2520unit%252C%2520in%2520order%2520to%2520express%2520the%2520absolute%2520or%2520corresponding%2520C.%2520G.%2520S.%2520magnetic%2520unit.%2522&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AInternational+System+of+Units"></span></span>
</li>
